Effect of an Unsuitable Condition as a Term in Kafalah
Ruling when an unsuitable condition is set as a deadline for kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a suspensive condition (ta'liq) and a term (ajal) in kafalah, and state the Hanafi ruling when an unsuitable condition is set as a term.

Core Idea
An unsuitable condition as a term is void, but the kafalah remains valid with immediate payment.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines a textual dispute regarding the effect of an unsuitable condition when it is made a deadline rather than a suspender. Al-Zaylai initially objected, but the correct position—followed in most copies—is that the contract stands, the term is ignored, and the money becomes due at once.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ta'liq from tajil
Identify whether the unsuitable condition is used to suspend the obligation (ta'liq) or simply set a deadline (tajil). This distinction is crucial: ta'liq makes the contract conditional; tajil only fixes a time frame.
The key is whether the condition is phrased as 'if' (in) or 'until' (ila an).
2
Understand al-Zaylai's objection
Al-Zaylai argued that the initial text in al-Hidayah was a slip: if a condition is unsuitable for ta'liq, it should also be unsuitable for tajil, making the contract invalid from the start.
He compared it to ta'liq on entering a house, which is invalid.
3
Learn the Hanafi response
The relied-upon position holds that when an unsuitable condition is made a term, the contract itself remains valid; only the void term is canceled. This is because the term does not suspend the contract's existence but only sets a future time, which can be ignored.
The contract is valid in itself; the void condition is stripped away.
4
Apply the analogy to marriage and manumission
The Hanafi response cites marriage and manumission as analogies: if a void condition is appended to a valid contract, the condition is ignored and the contract stands.
This reinforces the principle that a valid contract survives an invalid ancillary term.
5
Extend to kafalah bi al-nafs
The same rule applies to suretyship for a person: if an unsuitable condition is set as a term (e.g., 'until the rain comes'), the term is void and the surety must immediately produce the person.
Slight ambiguity in the term (like 'until harvest') is tolerated due to leniency in kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجلاً في الكفالة بأن يقول: كفلت بمال فلان إلى أن تهب الريح أو جاء المطر. تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً، هكذا وقع في "الهداية" و"الكافي"، قال الزيلعي وهو سهو؛ لأن الحكم فيه أن التعليق لا يصح، ولا يلزم المال؛ لأن الشرط غير ملائم، فصار كما لو علقه بدخول الدار ونحوه مما ليس بملائم، والثابت في أكثر النسخ هكذا: ولا يصح بنحو إن هبت الريح أو جاء المطر، وإن جعل أجلا تصح الكفالة، ويجب المال حالاً أي لو جعل هبوب الريح ونحوه من الشروط الغير ملائمة أجلا في الكفالة لا يصح التأجيل ويجب المال حالا، وصورة جعلهما أجلا أن يقول: كفلت به أو بما لك عليه إلى أن تهب الريح أو إلى أن يجيء المطر، ففي هذه الصورة تصح الكفالة ويجب المال حالاً؛ لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بأجل متعارف لم تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق، وكذلك الكفالة بالنفس يجوز تعليقها بشرط ملائم كالكفالة بالمال في جميع ما ذكرنا. ولا يجوز تعليقها بشرط غير ملائم، ويجوز تأجيلها إلى أجل معلوم والجهالة اليسيرة فيها متحملة كالتأجيل إلى القطاف وقدوم الحاج؛ لأن الكفالة يتسامح فيها ما لا يتسامح في غيرها، ولا يجوز تأجيل الكفالة بالنفس إلى هبوب الريح أو نزول المطر، فإن أجله إليه بطل الأجل ولزمه تسليم النفس حالاً.
Making it a term in kafalah, such as saying: 'I guarantee the money of so-and-so until the wind blows or the rain comes.' The kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately. This is how it appears in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. Al-Zaylai said it is a slip, because the ruling in that case (ta'liq) is that the suspension is invalid and the money is not due, because the condition is unsuitable, so it becomes like suspending on entering the house and the like which are not suitable. And the correct text in most copies is: 'And it is not valid with something like if the wind blows or rain comes, but if he makes it a term, the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately.' That is, if he makes the blowing of the wind and similar unsuitable conditions a term (ajal) in the kafalah, the term is invalid and the money is due immediately. The form of making them a term is when he says: 'I guarantee it, or what is owed to you, until the wind blows or until the rain comes.' In this case, the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately, because since the kafalah can be validly suspended on a customary term, it is not invalidated by invalid conditions, like divorce and manumission. Similarly, kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for a person) can be suspended on a suitable condition like kafalah bi al-mal in all we have mentioned. It is not permissible to suspend it on an unsuitable condition, and it is permissible to set a term for it up to a known time, and slight ignorance is tolerated, like setting a term until harvest time or the arrival of pilgrims, because kafalah is treated leniently in ways other contracts are not. It is not permissible to set a term for kafalah bi al-nafs until the wind blows or rain falls; if he sets it to that, the term is void and he must surrender the person immediately.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage deals with a textual issue in the Hidayah regarding the effect of an unsuitable condition when it is made a term (ajal) rather than a suspensive condition. The initial text in some copies stated that if one says 'I guarantee until the wind blows,' the kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately. Al-Zaylai criticized this as a slip, arguing that if the condition is unsuitable, the suspension is invalid altogether and no money is due. However, the correct position (and the one adopted in most copies) distinguishes between a suspensive condition (ta'liq) and a term (tajil). When the unsuitable condition is made a deadline, the contract is valid but the term is ignored, making the payment due immediately. This is because the kafalah contract itself is valid; only the void term is canceled, similar to void conditions in marriage or manumission. The commentary then extends this to kafalah bi al-nafs, where a suitable condition allows valid suspension, but an unsuitable condition as a term also results in immediate obligation. Slight ambiguity in the term (like 'until harvest') is tolerated due to the leniency of kafalah.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): A future time set as a deadline for payment or performance in a contract.
تأجيل (tajil): Setting a term for payment in a kafalah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says, 'I guarantee your debt until the rain comes,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The kafalah is invalid because the condition is unsuitable.
B) The kafalah is valid but the money is due only if it rains.
C) The kafalah is valid and the money is due immediately.
D) The kafalah is suspended until rain comes.

Key Takeaway
An unsuitable condition as a term in kafalah is void, but the contract remains valid and the payment is due immediately.

Ruling on Proof in Kafalah: Creditor's Claim and Surety's Denial
Evidentiary procedure in kafalah disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidentiary rules in a kafalah dispute and apply them to determine the surety's liability when the creditor claims and the surety denies.

Core Idea
The surety is bound only by proof or his own admission; the principal's admission does not bind the surety.

When a creditor claims a debt from a surety, the outcome hinges on whether the creditor can present evidence. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the precise procedures: if proof is provided, the surety must pay; otherwise, the surety's own admission and oath prevail. Understanding this distinction is critical for applying the Hanafi rules of evidence in suretyship cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
Recognize the creditor (al-talib/الطالب), the surety (al-kafil/الكفيل), and the principal debtor (al-makful 'anhu/المكفول عنه). The claim is for a debt that the surety guaranteed.
2
Analyze the effect of proof (bayyinah)
If the creditor presents evidence (two male witnesses) against the principal or the surety, the surety is bound for the proven amount. This proof is equivalent to the surety having personal knowledge of the debt.
The evidence can be presented in the principal's absence; judgment binds both.
3
Examine the surety's position when no proof is given
Without evidence, the surety's statement regarding the amount he admits is accepted, but he must take an oath on knowledge (yamin al-'ilm) that he does not know of a greater debt. This is not an oath of certainty because the debt belongs to another.
The surety is a defendant (munkir) regarding the excess, so his statement and oath prevail.
4
Understand the type of oath and its rationale
The oath is on knowledge, not on certainty, because the surety is swearing about what is due on another. The principle: 'the oath regarding what is due on another is only on knowledge.' This protects the surety from swearing falsely about a debt he may not know.
5
Determine the effect of the principal's admission
If the principal admits a larger debt, this does not affect the surety's liability. The principal's admission is only valid against himself, as he has no authority over the surety. The surety is bound only by his own admission or conclusive proof.
This is because one person's admission cannot impose liability on another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كفل بما له عليه، فبرهن على ألف لزمه، وإلا صدق الكفيل فيما أقر بحلفه، ولا ينفذ قول المطلوب به قليلاً كان أو كثيراً. يعني: إن أقام الطالب البينة على الأصيل أو على الكفيل عند غيبة الأصيل، ويكون ذلك قضاء على الحاضر والغائب، وإن لم يبرهن لا يجب عليه شيء؛ لأن قول الطالب لا يكون حجة على المطلوب، وهو المكفول عنه ولا على الكفيل؛ لأنه مدع، فلا يقبل قوله إلا ببينة. فإن برهن لزم الكفيل المال المكفول به؛ لأن الكفيل لو عاين ما عليه، وكفل عنه لزمه ما عليه، فكذلك إذا ثبت بالبينة. وإن لم يقم الطالب بينة كان القول قول الكفيل في قدر ما أقر مع اليمين على العلم بأني لا أعلم أن أكثر من هذا واجب على الأصيل لا على البتات؛ لأن الحلف فيما يجب على الغير ليس إلا على العلم. وإنما كان القول قوله؛ لأنه مال مجهول لزمه بقوله، وإنما كان مع اليمين؛ لأنه فيما كان خصماً فيه، هو والشيء مما يصح بدله يكون القول قوله مع اليمين كالمدعى عليه بالمال؛ ولأنه منكر للزيادة، والقول قول المنكر مع اليمين. ولا ينفذ قول المطلوب به على الكفيل، فإن قال المكفول عنه له علي ألف وقد أقر الكفيل بخمس مائة، ولا بينة للمكفول له لا يجب على الكفيل ألف؛ لأنه إقرار على الغير ولا ولاية له عليه، ويصدق المطلوب.
If he is surety for what is owed to him, and he proves a thousand, it is binding on him; otherwise, the surety is believed as to what he admits, with his oath; and the statement of the principal is not effective, whether little or much. Meaning: If the creditor presents evidence against the principal or against the surety when the principal is absent, that is a judgment against the present and absent. If he does not present evidence, nothing is due from him, because the creditor's statement is not a proof against the principal (the one guaranteed for) nor against the surety, since he is a claimant, so his statement is not accepted except with evidence. If he proves, the surety is bound by the guaranteed money, because if the surety had witnessed what was owed and became surety, he would be bound; similarly if it is proven by evidence. If the creditor does not present evidence, the surety's statement as to the amount he admits is accepted, with an oath on knowledge (that he does not know more is owed), not an oath of certainty, because oath regarding what is due on another is only on knowledge. The surety's statement is accepted because it is an unknown obligation that he is liable for by his own admission. It is with an oath because he is a defendant in a dispute over something fungible, and the defendant's statement is accepted with oath. The principal's statement does not affect the surety. If the principal says 'I owe him a thousand' and the surety admits five hundred, and the creditor has no evidence, the surety is not bound for a thousand, because it is an admission against another and the principal has no authority over the surety; the principal is believed [against himself].
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of evidence in a kafalah dispute. It begins with the case where the creditor (the principal's creditor) claims the debt. If the creditor provides proof (bayyinah) against either the principal or the surety (in the principal's absence), the surety is bound. This proof is considered as effective as if the surety had personal knowledge of the debt. If no proof is provided, the surety's statement regarding the amount he admits is accepted, and he must take an oath that he does not know of any greater amount being owed. This is because he is denying an increase, and the burden of proof is on the claimant. The oath is on knowledge (not on certainty) because the debt is owed by another. Finally, the principal's own admission of a larger debt does not affect the surety's liability; the principal's statement is only effective against himself, not against the surety, as it is an admission against a third party without authority.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two male witnesses, that the creditor must present to prove the debt.
يمين العلم (yamin al-'ilm): An oath on knowledge (that one does not know of a greater debt), not an oath of certainty (al-batt), because the debt is not directly the surety's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the creditor has no evidence and the surety admits 500 of a claimed 1000 debt, what is the ruling?
A) The surety must pay the full 1000 because the principal admitted it.
B) The surety is believed with an oath and pays only 500.
C) The case is dismissed because no proof.
D) The surety must pay 1000 if the creditor takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the surety is bound only by evidence or his own admission; the principal's admission does not affect the surety's liability.

Rights in Shared Walls: Nails, Windows, and Collapse
Explains lower owner's restricted rights in shared wall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on driving nails in shared walls, explain the intra-madhhab dispute, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A shared wall carries another's right; use without consent is restricted, but only harm prohibits for fatwa.

When a building is divided into upper and lower floors owned separately, the shared wall is not solely owned. The lower owner may want to drive a nail or open a window, but the upper owner has rights. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi approach, from Abu Hanifa's strict analogy to the Sahibayn's harm-based view, and the practical fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Prohibition
The matn states the lower owner cannot drive a nail or open a window without the upper owner's consent. Recognize the specific actions: driving a nail (watad) and opening a window (kuwwah).
2
Understand the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifa prohibits absolutely because the wall is like a pledge (rahn) with another's right. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) prohibit only if harm occurs.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the Qiyas of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa analogizes the wall to a pledge (rahn) or leased property ('ayn musta'jara) where the owner cannot dispose without consent. The illa (effective cause) is the existence of another's right in the same object.
This is a qiyas jaliy, as the illa is clear.
4
Contrast the Sahibayn's Ownership-Based Reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that ownership implies freedom of use unless proven harmful. They reject the pledge analogy because the other's right is not as strong; it is a right to support and stability, not exclusive use.
They consider the owner's right primary.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Its Practical Effect
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the Sahibayn's: the lower owner may act if no harm is caused. This affects daily practice, allowing harmless nails but prohibiting damaging openings.
Fatawa 'Alamgiri and later works adopt this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء مسائل شتی لا يتد ذو سفل فيه، ولا ينقب كوّة بلا رضاء ذي العلو. زائغة مستطيلة لا تنشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا، بخلاف المستديرة. شتى: أي متفرقة هو جمع شتيت كجرحى وجريح، وهو مرفوع على الوصفية للمسائل، وإذا قلت: جاء القوم شتى يكون منصوبا على الحال أي متفرقين، وهذا من دأب المصنفين أن يذكروا في آخر الكتاب مسائل متعلقة بما قبلها استدراكاً لما فات من الكتاب ويترجمونه بمسائل شتى أو منثورة أو متفرقة. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) لا يتد [ أي إذا كان العلو لرجل والسفل للآخر لا يدق وتدا. (عيني) من "وتد" يتد" من باب ضرب أصله "يوتد" حذفت الواو؛ لوقوعها بين الياء والكسرة كما في "يعد" أصله "يوعد" والمعنى لا يدق، وفي "المغرب": وتد الوتد ضربه بالميتدة، والوتد قطعة من الخشب أو الحديد يدق في الحائط ليعلق عليه شيء. (عيني، فتح) ذو سفل: بكسر السين وضمها ضد العلو وصورته أن يكون بيت وفوقه طبقة كل واحد لرجل، فليس لصاحب البيت التحتاني أن يدق فيه وتداً. ولا ينقب كوة بضم الكاف وتشديد الواو، وهي الطاقة، وبفتح الكاف وهي الروزن أي ليس لصاحب السفل أن ينقب فيه كوة. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) بلا رضاء ذي العلو: مطلقاً عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان الوتد أو النقب مضرين أو لا؛ لأنه تصرف في محل تعلق به حق الغير كالرهن وقالا يصنع فيه ما لا يضر بالعلو، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أراد صاحب العلو أن يضع على العلو شيئاً، أو يضع عليه جذوعاً، أو يحدث كنيفاً، قيل: ما حكي عنهما تفسير لقول أبي حنيفة على معنى أنه يمنع إلا ما فيه ضرر مثل ما قالا، وقيل فيه خلاف حقيقة، وهو أن الأصل عندهما الإباحة؛ لأنه تصرف في ملكه والملك يقتضي إطلاق التصرف فلا يمنع عنه إلا بعارض الضرر، وعنده الأصل هو الحظر؛ لأنه تصرف في محل تعلق به حق الغير كالرهن والعين المستأجرة، ولو انهدم السفل من غير صنع صاحبه، لا يجبر على البناء؛ لعدم التعدي، ولكن لصاحب العلو أن يبني إن شاء، ويبني عليه علوه. ثم يرجع بقيمة البناء عليه، ويمنعه من السكنى فيه حتى يدفع إليه قيمته يوم البناء؛ لأنه مضطر في ذلك، بخلاف الدار المشتركة إذا انهدمت، فبناها أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه حيث لا يرجع؛ لأنه متبرع؛ إذ هو ليس مضطر؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقسم عرصتها ويبني في نصيبه، وصاحب العلو ليس كذلك، حتى لو كانت الدار صغيرة بحيث لا يمكن الانتفاع بنصيبه بعد القسمة، كان له أن يرجع، ولو هدم صاحب السفل سفله بنفسه يجبر على إعادته لتعديه بمحل تعلق به حق الغير كالراهن يعتق العبد المرهون. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) زائغة : [ أي سكة من زاغت الشمس إذا مالت تسمى المحلة والسكة زائغة؛ لميلها من طرف إلى طرف. (عيني)] سواء كانت نافذة أو غير نافذة ولابد من تقييد الثانية المنشعبة بكونها غير نافذة، وفي تهذيب ديوان الأدب": الزائغة: الطريقة التي حادت عن الطريق الأعظم (عيني، فتح) مستطيلة صفة زائغة أي طويلة من استطال بمعنى طال. (عيني) لا تنشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا، بخلاف المستديرة. تتفرع عن الزائغة المستطيلة أي الزائغة الأولى أي السكة الأخرى للمرور مثلها: أي مثل الزائغة زائغة أخرى طولاً. (عيني) غير نافذة: إلى موضع آخر ولا له طريق غير الطريق الزائغة والمستطيلة. (عيني) لا يفتح أهل الأولى: لأن الباب يقصد للمرور، ولا حق لهم في الدخول فيها؛ لكونها غير نافذة، وإنما ذلك لأهلها على الخصوص، ألا ترى أنه لو بيعت دار منها كان حق الشفعة لهم لا لأهل الأولى بحق الشركة في الطريق، بخلاف أهل القصوى فإن لأحدهم أن يفتح بابا في الأولى؛ لأن له حق المرور فيها وبخلاف ما إذا كانت الزائغة المنشعبة نافذة؛ لأن حق المرور للعامة وهم من جملتهم، فلأهل الأولى أن يفتحوا الباب فيها، وقيل: لا يمنعون من فتح الباب؛ لأن الفتح رفع لبعض جداره، وله أن يرفع جميع جداره بالهدم فرفع بعضه أولى، والصحيح هو الأول، ولكن إذا أراد أن يفتح الباب للمرور فإنه يمنع استحساناً، وإذا أراد به الاستضاءة أو الريح دون المرور لم يمنع من ذلك، وقيل: يمنع أيضاً؛ لأنه بعد تركيب الباب يدعي حق المرور على طول الزمان فيستدل بالباب على أن له حق المرور فيحكم له به. (عيني، مسكين، فتح) فيه بابا أي الزائغة المنشعبة وتذكير الضمير باعتبار لفظ "مثلها". (عيني) بخلاف المستديرة: يعني إذا كانت الزائغة المنشعبة مستديرة يجوز لأهل الزائغة المستطيلة فتح الباب؛ لأنها لما كانت مستديرة، وهي التي فيها اعوجاج حتى بلغ اعوجاجها رأس السكة صارت كلتاهما سكة واحدة وهي بينهم على الشركة، حتى إذا بيع دار فيها حق الشفعة، وهذا إذا كانت المستديرة غير نافذة أيضاً، وإن كانت نافذة فلجميع المسلمين فيها حق المرور، وقدر الاستدارة مثل نصف دائرة أو أقل حتى لو كانت أكبر من ذلك لا يفتح فيها. واعلم أن للإنسان أن يتصرف في ملكه ما شاء ما لم يضر بغيره ضرراً ظاهراً، فيجوز له أن يتخذ في داره حماماً، وعن أبي يوسف إن تأذى الجيران بدخانه فلهم منعه إلا أن يكون دخانه مثل دخانهم، وأن يتخذ حظيرة غنم، وإن تأذى الجيران من نتن السرقين، وأن يحفر بئراً، وإن كان حائط جاره ينز منها، وقيل: إن كان يعلم يقيناً أنه منه، فله منعه، ولا يجوز أن يبني في داره تنوراً للخبز الدائم كما في السوق، أو يبني رحى للطحن، أو مدقا استحساناً؛ لأن ذلك يضر ضررا فاحشاً لا يمكن التحرز عنه [ رمز الحقائق: ۱۳۱/۲] (مسكين)
Book of Judiciary. Miscellaneous issues. The owner of the lower floor shall not drive a nail in it, nor shall he break open a window without the consent of the owner of the upper floor. (Explanation of 'shall not drive a nail': If the upper part belongs to one person and the lower to another, he shall not drive a nail. 'Owner of the lower floor': his house is below and there is a floor above, each belonging to a different person, so the owner of the lower house may not drive a nail in it. 'Nor break open a window': the lower owner may not break open a window. 'Without consent of the upper owner': According to Abu Hanifa, absolutely, whether the nail or opening causes harm or not, because it is a disposal in a place that has a right of another attached to it, like a pledge. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he may do what does not harm the upper. If the upper owner wants to place something on his part, there is a disagreement. ... A long and winding alley: a lane that deviates from the main road. A winding and elongated alley from which branches another similar alley that is not a thoroughfare: the residents of the first alley may not open a door in it, unlike the circular alley. (Commentary explaining conditions, rights of passage, and general rules about property use without harming others.)
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents rulings on property rights when a building is divided into upper and lower floors owned by different persons, and also on alleys that are winding and branching. The matn states that the lower owner cannot drive a nail or open a window in the shared structure without the upper owner's consent. The commentary (from Fath al-Qadir and others) explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, this prohibition is absolute—even if no harm is caused—because the wall is a place where the other owner has a right. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) restrict the prohibition to cases where actual harm occurs. This disagreement extends to other actions by the upper owner, like placing beams or constructing a toilet. The commentary then discusses the scenario where the lower floor collapses: the lower owner is not forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may rebuild and demand repayment, and prevent the lower owner from living there until paid, unlike co-owned property. Then it addresses alleys: if a long winding alley branches into another similar alley that is a dead end, residents of the first alley may not open a door into the branch, because they have no right of passage there. However, if the branch is circular, both alleys are considered shared, so they may open doors. The passage concludes with general Hanafi rules: a person may use his property as he wishes as long as it does not cause manifest harm to others, with specific examples like baths (smoke) and wells.

Key Terms
وَتِد (watad): A nail or peg driven into a wall to hang something
كَوَّة (kuwwah): An opening or window in a wall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, can the owner of a lower floor drive a harmless nail into the shared wall without the upper owner's permission?
A) No, absolutely prohibited.
B) Yes, allowed because no harm is caused.
C) Only if the nail is small.
D) It depends on the upper owner's gender.

Key Takeaway
In shared walls, the lower owner may use the wall without consent if no harm results; the mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn's harm-based approach.

Rights in Winding Alleys: Opening Doors in Dead-End Lanes
Explains rights of opening doors in branching alleys.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dead-end from circular alleys in Hanafi property law and apply the rule on opening doors.

Core Idea
In a dead-end branch alley, only its residents have access; outsiders may not open doors into it.

Alleys often branch into narrower lanes. Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between dead-end (ghayr nafidha) and thoroughfare (nafidha) alleys. The shape matters: a long winding alley that branches into another similar non-through alley is private to its residents; others cannot open new doors into it. But a circular alley is considered shared. This lesson examines these rules and their practical effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Alley Types
The passage discusses a 'za'igha' (winding alley). It can be nafidha (through) or ghayr nafidha (dead-end). Here, the branch is non-nafidha.
Za'igha means winding, from zaghā 'the sun moved'.
2
Apply the Rule for Non-Through Branches
If a long winding alley (al-ūla) has a branch (mānsha'ba) that is also winding and non-through, the residents of the first alley cannot open a door into the branch.
The reason: they have no right of passage in a private dead-end lane.
3
Understand the Exception for Circular Alleys
If the branch is circular (mustadīra), it is considered one shared alley. Then residents of the first alley may open doors.
Circular means the alley curves back, making it a single unit.
4
Consider the Minor Ikhtilaf on Purpose of Opening
There is a dispute: if a resident wants to open a door for light or air (not passage), some say it is allowed, others say it may still be prohibited because the door could later be used for passage.
The reliable view permits it for non-passage purposes.
5
Apply the General Property Principle
The passage concludes: a person may use his property as he wishes as long as no manifest harm. This underpins the alley rulings.
This principle is a key Hanafi maxim.

Source Text

كتاب القضاء مسائل شتی لا يتد ذو سفل فيه، ولا ينقب كوّة بلا رضاء ذي العلو. زائغة مستطيلة لا تنشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا، بخلاف المستديرة. شتى: أي متفرقة هو جمع شتيت كجرحى وجريح، وهو مرفوع على الوصفية للمسائل، وإذا قلت: جاء القوم شتى يكون منصوبا على الحال أي متفرقين، وهذا من دأب المصنفين أن يذكروا في آخر الكتاب مسائل متعلقة بما قبلها استدراكاً لما فات من الكتاب ويترجمونه بمسائل شتى أو منثورة أو متفرقة. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) لا يتد [ أي إذا كان العلو لرجل والسفل للآخر لا يدق وتدا. (عيني) من "وتد" يتد" من باب ضرب أصله "يوتد" حذفت الواو؛ لوقوعها بين الياء والكسرة كما في "يعد" أصله "يوعد" والمعنى لا يدق، وفي "المغرب": وتد الوتد ضربه بالميتدة، والوتد قطعة من الخشب أو الحديد يدق في الحائط ليعلق عليه شيء. (عيني، فتح) ذو سفل: بكسر السين وضمها ضد العلو وصورته أن يكون بيت وفوقه طبقة كل واحد لرجل، فليس لصاحب البيت التحتاني أن يدق فيه وتداً. ولا ينقب كوة بضم الكاف وتشديد الواو، وهي الطاقة، وبفتح الكاف وهي الروزن أي ليس لصاحب السفل أن ينقب فيه كوة. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) بلا رضاء ذي العلو: مطلقاً عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان الوتد أو النقب مضرين أو لا؛ لأنه تصرف في محل تعلق به حق الغير كالرهن وقالا يصنع فيه ما لا يضر بالعلو، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أراد صاحب العلو أن يضع على العلو شيئاً، أو يضع عليه جذوعاً، أو يحدث كنيفاً، قيل: ما حكي عنهما تفسير لقول أبي حنيفة على معنى أنه يمنع إلا ما فيه ضرر مثل ما قالا، وقيل فيه خلاف حقيقة، وهو أن الأصل عندهما الإباحة؛ لأنه تصرف في ملكه والملك يقتضي إطلاق التصرف فلا يمنع عنه إلا بعارض الضرر، وعنده الأصل هو الحظر؛ لأنه تصرف في محل تعلق به حق الغير كالرهن والعين المستأجرة، ولو انهدم السفل من غير صنع صاحبه، لا يجبر على البناء؛ لعدم التعدي، ولكن لصاحب العلو أن يبني إن شاء، ويبني عليه علوه. ثم يرجع بقيمة البناء عليه، ويمنعه من السكنى فيه حتى يدفع إليه قيمته يوم البناء؛ لأنه مضطر في ذلك، بخلاف الدار المشتركة إذا انهدمت، فبناها أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه حيث لا يرجع؛ لأنه متبرع؛ إذ هو ليس مضطر؛ لأنه يمكنه أن يقسم عرصتها ويبني في نصيبه، وصاحب العلو ليس كذلك، حتى لو كانت الدار صغيرة بحيث لا يمكن الانتفاع بنصيبه بعد القسمة، كان له أن يرجع، ولو هدم صاحب السفل سفله بنفسه يجبر على إعادته لتعديه بمحل تعلق به حق الغير كالراهن يعتق العبد المرهون. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٠/٢ (فتح) زائغة : [ أي سكة من زاغت الشمس إذا مالت تسمى المحلة والسكة زائغة؛ لميلها من طرف إلى طرف. (عيني)] سواء كانت نافذة أو غير نافذة ولابد من تقييد الثانية المنشعبة بكونها غير نافذة، وفي تهذيب ديوان الأدب": الزائغة: الطريقة التي حادت عن الطريق الأعظم (عيني، فتح) مستطيلة صفة زائغة أي طويلة من استطال بمعنى طال. (عيني) لا تنشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيه بابا، بخلاف المستديرة. تتفرع عن الزائغة المستطيلة أي الزائغة الأولى أي السكة الأخرى للمرور مثلها: أي مثل الزائغة زائغة أخرى طولاً. (عيني) غير نافذة: إلى موضع آخر ولا له طريق غير الطريق الزائغة والمستطيلة. (عيني) لا يفتح أهل الأولى: لأن الباب يقصد للمرور، ولا حق لهم في الدخول فيها؛ لكونها غير نافذة، وإنما ذلك لأهلها على الخصوص، ألا ترى أنه لو بيعت دار منها كان حق الشفعة لهم لا لأهل الأولى بحق الشركة في الطريق، بخلاف أهل القصوى فإن لأحدهم أن يفتح بابا في الأولى؛ لأن له حق المرور فيها وبخلاف ما إذا كانت الزائغة المنشعبة نافذة؛ لأن حق المرور للعامة وهم من جملتهم، فلأهل الأولى أن يفتحوا الباب فيها، وقيل: لا يمنعون من فتح الباب؛ لأن الفتح رفع لبعض جداره، وله أن يرفع جميع جداره بالهدم فرفع بعضه أولى، والصحيح هو الأول، ولكن إذا أراد أن يفتح الباب للمرور فإنه يمنع استحساناً، وإذا أراد به الاستضاءة أو الريح دون المرور لم يمنع من ذلك، وقيل: يمنع أيضاً؛ لأنه بعد تركيب الباب يدعي حق المرور على طول الزمان فيستدل بالباب على أن له حق المرور فيحكم له به. (عيني، مسكين، فتح) فيه بابا أي الزائغة المنشعبة وتذكير الضمير باعتبار لفظ "مثلها". (عيني) بخلاف المستديرة: يعني إذا كانت الزائغة المنشعبة مستديرة يجوز لأهل الزائغة المستطيلة فتح الباب؛ لأنها لما كانت مستديرة، وهي التي فيها اعوجاج حتى بلغ اعوجاجها رأس السكة صارت كلتاهما سكة واحدة وهي بينهم على الشركة، حتى إذا بيع دار فيها حق الشفعة، وهذا إذا كانت المستديرة غير نافذة أيضاً، وإن كانت نافذة فلجميع المسلمين فيها حق المرور، وقدر الاستدارة مثل نصف دائرة أو أقل حتى لو كانت أكبر من ذلك لا يفتح فيها. واعلم أن للإنسان أن يتصرف في ملكه ما شاء ما لم يضر بغيره ضرراً ظاهراً، فيجوز له أن يتخذ في داره حماماً، وعن أبي يوسف إن تأذى الجيران بدخانه فلهم منعه إلا أن يكون دخانه مثل دخانهم، وأن يتخذ حظيرة غنم، وإن تأذى الجيران من نتن السرقين، وأن يحفر بئراً، وإن كان حائط جاره ينز منها، وقيل: إن كان يعلم يقيناً أنه منه، فله منعه، ولا يجوز أن يبني في داره تنوراً للخبز الدائم كما في السوق، أو يبني رحى للطحن، أو مدقا استحساناً؛ لأن ذلك يضر ضررا فاحشاً لا يمكن التحرز عنه [ رمز الحقائق: ۱۳۱/۲] (مسكين)
Book of Judiciary. Miscellaneous issues. The owner of the lower floor shall not drive a nail in it, nor shall he break open a window without the consent of the owner of the upper floor. (Explanation of 'shall not drive a nail': If the upper part belongs to one person and the lower to another, he shall not drive a nail. 'Owner of the lower floor': his house is below and there is a floor above, each belonging to a different person, so the owner of the lower house may not drive a nail in it. 'Nor break open a window': the lower owner may not break open a window. 'Without consent of the upper owner': According to Abu Hanifa, absolutely, whether the nail or opening causes harm or not, because it is a disposal in a place that has a right of another attached to it, like a pledge. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he may do what does not harm the upper. If the upper owner wants to place something on his part, there is a disagreement. ... A long and winding alley: a lane that deviates from the main road. A winding and elongated alley from which branches another similar alley that is not a thoroughfare: the residents of the first alley may not open a door in it, unlike the circular alley. (Commentary explaining conditions, rights of passage, and general rules about property use without harming others.)
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents rulings on property rights when a building is divided into upper and lower floors owned by different persons, and also on alleys that are winding and branching. The matn states that the lower owner cannot drive a nail or open a window in the shared structure without the upper owner's consent. The commentary (from Fath al-Qadir and others) explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, this prohibition is absolute—even if no harm is caused—because the wall is a place where the other owner has a right. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) restrict the prohibition to cases where actual harm occurs. This disagreement extends to other actions by the upper owner, like placing beams or constructing a toilet. The commentary then discusses the scenario where the lower floor collapses: the lower owner is not forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may rebuild and demand repayment, and prevent the lower owner from living there until paid, unlike co-owned property. Then it addresses alleys: if a long winding alley branches into another similar alley that is a dead end, residents of the first alley may not open a door into the branch, because they have no right of passage there. However, if the branch is circular, both alleys are considered shared, so they may open doors. The passage concludes with general Hanafi rules: a person may use his property as he wishes as long as it does not cause manifest harm to others, with specific examples like baths (smoke) and wells.

Key Terms
زَائِغَة (zā'ighah): A winding alley or lane that deviates from the main road

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a long winding dead-end alley (main), if a branch is also non-through and similar in shape, what is the Hanafi ruling for residents of the main alley opening a door into that branch?
A) They may open a door freely because they own the wall.
B) They may not open a door because the branch is private and they have no right of passage.
C) They may open a door only if the branch is circular.
D) They may open a door if they pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
In dead-end branch alleys, only the branch residents have access; outsiders cannot open doors unless the branch is circular or a thoroughfare.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Seller's Intercourse After Buyer's Denial of Purchase
Ruling of the seller's intercourse after buyer's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the seller's intercourse after the buyer's denial and identify the conditions that make it permissible.

Core Idea
Buyer's denial plus seller's abandonment of lawsuit rescinds the sale, permitting the seller intercourse.

In classical Hanafi sales law, when a seller claims a buyer purchased a slave-girl and the buyer denies, the contract is effectively rescinded by the denial. However, the seller cannot immediately reclaim the slave-girl physically; he must first abandon the lawsuit. This lesson examines the precise ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and denial
The seller asserts a sale occurred; the buyer denies it. This denial is treated as a rescission in Hanafi law.
Denial (juhūd) is considered a form of rescission by istiḥsān.
2
Determine the legal effect of denial
Hanafis hold that the buyer's denial effectively cancels the contract, returning ownership to the seller.
Other schools may require a court ruling.
3
Identify the condition for seller's action
The seller must abandon the lawsuit (tark al-khuṣūmah) to confirm the rescission. Abandonment can be by intention or explicit statement.
Without abandonment, the contract is still pending.
4
State the ruling and additional condition
The seller may then have intercourse if he also performs istibrā' (wait one menstrual cycle) to ensure no pregnancy from the buyer.
Istibrā' is required in any case of uncertain ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال لآخر: اشتريت منّي هذه الأمة فأنكر، للبائع أن يطأها إن ترك الخصومة.
And whoever said to another: You bought this slave-girl from me, and he (the other) denied, then the seller may have intercourse with her if he abandons the lawsuit.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This ruling deals with a sale contract where the seller claims the buyer purchased a slave-girl, but the buyer denies. The Hanafi position: if the seller abandons the lawsuit (i.e., does not pursue the claim in court), it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with the slave-girl. This is because the buyer's denial is considered a tacit rescission of the contract (juhūd is a form of rescission). By abandoning the lawsuit, the seller implicitly agrees to the rescission. The contract is thus rescinded, and ownership reverts to the seller. However, before intercourse, the seller must perform istibrā’ (wait for one menstrual cycle) to ensure she is not pregnant from the buyer. After that, intercourse is lawful. Also, the seller may not return the slave-girl to a previous seller due to defects, as the rescission is by mutual consent.

Key Terms
ترك الخصومة (tark al-khuṣūmah): Abandoning the lawsuit or claim in court, either by intention or declaration.
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave; the object of sale in this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the seller do before having intercourse with the slave-girl after the buyer denies the purchase?
A) Obtain a court order.
B) Wait for istibrā’ (one menstrual cycle).
C) Notify the buyer.
D) Repay the purchase price to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The seller may have intercourse with the slave-girl only after abandoning the lawsuit and performing istibrā'.

Denial as Rescission: Seller's Intercourse After Buyer Denies Purchase
Ruling on seller's intercourse after buyer denies purchase, with legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the buyer's denial of purchase constitutes a rescission of the contract, and apply the conditions for the seller to lawfully have intercourse with the slave-girl.

Core Idea
The buyer's denial is a metaphorical rescission, allowing the seller to have intercourse after dropping the lawsuit and observing istibra.

This lesson explains a unique Hanafi ruling in sales: when a buyer denies purchasing a slave-girl, his denial is treated as a rescission of the contract. The seller can then complete the rescission by dropping the lawsuit and take the slave-girl back. After observing istibra, he may have intercourse with her. We will explore the legal reasoning behind treating denial as rescission and the conditions that must be met.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Consider a sale: one man claims another bought a slave-girl for 1000 dirhams, but the second man denies the purchase. This denial is the starting point.
2
Grasp the metaphor of juhud as faskh
The Hanafi school treats denial (juhud) as a metaphorical rescission (faskh). Both reject the contract from its origin; thus the buyer's denial itself creates a rescission from his side.
The text uses the term 'isti'ara' (metaphor) to justify the legal equivalence.
3
Identify the conditions for the seller's intercourse
The seller must actively drop the lawsuit (either by intention or verbal statement according to disagreement) and perform an act indicating satisfaction with rescission (e.g., taking the slave-girl into his possession). After that, he must observe istibra (one menstrual cycle) before intercourse.
If the slave-girl was in the buyer's possession, the seller should wait for istibra to ensure she is not pregnant from the buyer.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement on 'dropping the lawsuit'
Some Hanafi scholars say mere intention (bi al-qalb) suffices, while others require a verbal testimony (bi al-lisan). The text presents both views without preferring one.
This difference does not affect the permissibility of intercourse, only how the seller manifests his consent.
5
Recognize the consequence: buyer's later evidence rejected
Once the rescission is complete by mutual consent (the buyer's denial and the seller's dropping), if the buyer later produces evidence that he actually bought her, it is not accepted. The contract is considered rescinded.
This prevents the buyer from reviving the contract after rescission, protecting the seller's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخصومة: اختلف في معنى ترك الخصومة فقيل: يكتفي بالقلب، وقيل: يشهد بلسانه على ما في قلبه يعني لو أن رجلا قال لآخر: اشتريت مني هذه الجارية بألف درهم وأنكر الآخر وقال ما اشتريتها فإن عزم البائع على ترك الخصومة يحل للبائع وطء الجارية؛ لأن المشتري لما جحد الشراء كان ذلك فسخاً منه؛ إذ الجحود كناية عن الفسخ؛ لأن الفسخ رفع العقد من الأصل، والجحود إنكار للعقد من الأصل فكان بينهما مناسبة، فجازت الاستعارة، فكان فسخاً من جهته، فإذا ساعده البائع بترك الخصومة، واقترن تركه بفعل يدل على الرضاء بالفسخ كإمساكها ونقلها إلى منزله تم الفسخ فحل له وطئها بعد الاستبراء إن كانت في يد المشتري، وليس له أن يردها على بائعها الأول بالعيب إن وجد بها عيباً قديماً بعد ذلك؛ لتمام الفسخ بالتراضي، حتى إذا أقام المشتري بعد ذلك بينة أنه اشتراها منه بعد ذلك لا تقبل بينته [ رمز الحقائق: ١٣٢/٢] (فتح، عناية)
The lawsuit: there is disagreement on the meaning of dropping the lawsuit: some say intention is sufficient, others say he must testify with his tongue to what is in his heart. Meaning, if a man said to another: You bought this slave-girl from me for a thousand dirhams, and the other denied and said: I did not buy her, then if the seller resolves to drop the lawsuit, it becomes permissible for the seller to have intercourse with the slave-girl; because when the buyer denied the purchase, that was a rescission from him, since denial is a metaphor for rescission; because rescission is the lifting of the contract from its origin, and denial is the rejection of the contract from its origin, so between them there is suitability, so the metaphor is permissible. Thus it is a rescission from his side. So when the seller assists him by dropping the lawsuit, and his dropping is accompanied by an act indicating satisfaction with the rescission, such as keeping her and moving her to his home, the rescission is completed, and it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her after istibra if she was in the buyer's possession. And he does not have the right to return her to her first seller due to a defect if a pre-existing defect is found afterwards, because the rescission is complete by mutual consent. Even if the buyer later produces evidence that he bought her from him after that, his evidence is not accepted. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 132/2] (Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in sales: a buyer denies purchasing a slave-girl (or any commodity), and the seller drops the lawsuit. The author explains the legal reasoning: the buyer's denial of the contract is treated as a rescission (faskh) because denial effectively rejects the contract from its origin, similar to rescission. When the seller also drops the lawsuit and performs an act indicating consent (like taking possession), the rescission is complete by mutual consent. Once rescission is complete, the seller is allowed to have intercourse with the slave-girl after observing istibra (a waiting period to ensure she is not pregnant from the buyer). However, if the buyer later provides evidence that he actually bought her, that evidence is not accepted because the contract was already rescinded. The passage also mentions a disagreement on what constitutes 'dropping the lawsuit': some say mere intention is enough, others require verbal testimony.

Key Terms
الجحود (al-juhud): Denial; rejecting the existence of a contract or debt.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract from its inception, as if it never existed.
الاستبراء (al-istibra): A waiting period for a slave-girl after acquisition or before intercourse to ensure she is not pregnant from a previous master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the buyer's denial of purchase treated as a rescission in Hanafi law?
A) Because denial is a form of lying.
B) Because denial is metaphorically equivalent to rescission, both rejecting the contract from its origin.
C) Because the seller wishes to rescind.
D) Because the judge orders it.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's denial of purchase in a sales contract is treated as a rescission (faskh) in Hanafi law, allowing the seller to reclaim the object and, after dropping the lawsuit and observing istibra, lawfully have intercourse.

Claim of Counterfeit Dirhams After Acknowledging Receipt
Ruling on believing claim of counterfeit dirhams after receipt acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between zuyuf, nubahrja, and mustawqa dirhams and apply the Hanafi rules regarding acknowledgment and subsequent claims, differentiating between connected and disconnected statements.

Core Idea
The term 'dirham' includes various types, but mustawqa coins are not considered dirhams, so claims about them after receipt are not believed if made separately.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines a common commercial scenario: someone acknowledges receiving ten dirhams, then later claims they were counterfeit. The ruling hinges on the type of dirham and whether the claim is made in the same breath or later. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for accurately applying Hanafi contract law regarding payment and dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the general rule
The passage begins: 'Whoever acknowledges receipt of ten (dirhams) then claims they are counterfeit, he is believed with his oath.' Note that this is the default ruling. The commentary then explains conditions.
The general rule applies to zuyuf and nubahrja, but not mustawqa under certain conditions.
2
Distinguish the three types of dirhams
Define zuyuf (rejected by treasury), nubahrja (rejected by merchants), and mustawqa (predominantly adulterated). Understand that zuyuf and nubahrja are still called dirhams, but mustawqa is not.
The key is that mustawqa is excluded from the term 'dirham' due to excessive adulteration.
3
Understand the muwasul vs mafsul distinction
A statement is muwasul (connected) if the acknowledgment and claim are made without a break; mafsul (disconnected) if separated. The commentary notes that the ruling applies whether connected or disconnected for zuyuf and nubahrja, but not for mustawqa when disconnected.
The author used 'then' (ثم) to indicate delay, showing the ruling even applies disconnectedly.
4
Apply the rule to each type
For zuyuf and nubahrja: believed with oath in both muwasul and mafsul. For mustawqa: believed only if muwasul; not believed if mafsul because the term dirham does not include mustawqa.
If the acknowledgment explicitly mentions 'good dirhams', then later claiming counterfeit is not believed in either case.
5
Consider the special case of explicit quality acknowledgment
If someone says 'I received good dirhams', then claims they are counterfeit, the claim is contradictory. If the acknowledgment was of 'my right' or 'the price', it is considered acknowledgment of amount and quality together, so excluding quality is valid when muwasul, like saying 'I owe a thousand except a hundred'.
The distinction is based on whether the quality was included in the acknowledgment or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أقرّ بقبض عشرة ثم ادعى أنها زيوف، صدق مع يمينه. ومن أقر بقبض إلخ: أي من أقر بقبض عشرة دراهم من رجل قرضاً أو ثمن السلعة له عنده أو غير ذلك، ثم أنه زيوف أو نبهرجة صدق مع يمينه سواء قال ذلك موصولاً أو مفصولاً، ولهذا عبر بــ"بـ"ثم" الدالة على التراخي وعدم الوصل؛ ليعلم الحكم في الوصل بالأولى؛ لأنه إذا صدق في المفصول، ففي الموصول بالأولى؛ أن ووجهه اسم الدراهم يقع على الجياد والزيوف والنبهرجة، ولهذا لو تجوز بهما في الصرف والسلم جاز، والقبض لا يختص بالجياد، فيصدق في إنكاره قبض حقه مع يمينه وقيد بالزيوف؛ لأنه لو قال: إنها ستوقة، لا يصدق مفصولاً، وأما موصولاً، فيصدق في الكل، وإنما لا يصدق في ستوقة مفصولاً؛ لأن اسم الدراهم لا يقع عليها، حتى لا يجوز التجوز بها؛ لغلبة الغش عليها. والزيوف ما يرده بيت المال، والنبهرجة: ما يرده التجار، وقيل: هي التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان وعلى كل قول لم يعتبر في الزيوف والنبهرجة غلبة الغش والستوقة: ما يغلب عليه الغش، وقيل: هي صفر مموه. والحاصل: أنه إن قال: قبضت دراهم جياداً، لا يصدق في دعواه الزيوف مطلقاً، سواء كان موصولاً أو مفصولاً، وفيما إذا أقر أنه قبض الثمن أو حقه أو استوفى، ثم ادعى أنها زيوف ينظر ، فإن كان مفصولاً لا يصدق، وإن كان موصولاً يصدق، ووجه الفرق أن قوله قبضت ما لي عليه أو حقي إقرار بقبض القدر والجودة بلفظ واحد، فإذا استثنى الجودة فقد استثنى البعض من الجملة، فيصح موصولاً، كما لو قال علي ألف إلا مائة، أما إذا قال: قبضت عشرة جياداً بلفظ على حدة، فإذا قال إلا أنها زيوف فقد استثنى الكل من الكل في حق الجودة، وذلك باطل وإن كان موصولاً.
And whoever acknowledges receipt of ten (dirhams) then claims they are counterfeit, he is believed with his oath. (Commentary:) And whoever acknowledges receipt etc.: meaning whoever acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams from a man as a loan or as a price of goods in his possession or otherwise, then (claims) that they are counterfeit or nubahrja, he is believed with his oath, whether he says that connected or disconnected. Therefore he (the author) expressed with "then" indicating delay and lack of connectivity; to make the ruling in the connected case known by priority; because if he is believed in the disconnected, then in the connected even more so. And the reason is that the term "dirham" applies to good, counterfeit, and nubahrja, and therefore if one transacts with them in sarf and salam it is valid, and receipt is not specific to good ones, so he is believed in denying receipt of his right with his oath. And he restricted (the ruling) to counterfeit; because if he says: they are mustawqa, he is not believed when disconnected, but when connected he is believed in all. And he is only not believed in mustawqa when disconnected because the term "dirham" does not apply to them, so transacting with them is not permissible due to the prevalence of adulteration. Counterfeit (zuyuf) is what the treasury rejects; nubahrja is what merchants reject; and it is said they are those minted outside the ruler's mint. And in all sayings, prevalence of adulteration is not considered in zuyuf and nubahrja. Mustawqa is that in which adulteration prevails; and it is said they are brass plated. The conclusion: that if he says: I received good dirhams, he is not believed in his claim of counterfeit absolutely, whether connected or disconnected. And in the case where he acknowledges that he received the price or his right or took it, then claims they are counterfeit, it is considered: if disconnected, he is not believed; if connected, he is believed. The distinguishing point is that his saying "I received what is owed to me or my right" is an acknowledgment of receipt of amount and quality in one expression. So if he excludes quality, he has excluded part from the whole, so it is valid when connected, like if he said: I owe a thousand except a hundred. But if he said: I received ten good dirhams in a separate expression, then says except that they are counterfeit, he has excluded the whole from the whole regarding quality, and that is invalid even if connected.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling about a person who acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams then claims they were counterfeit. The commentator explains the conditions under which the claim is accepted. The general rule is that the claim is accepted with the claimant's oath, whether the statement is connected (muwasul) or disconnected (mafsul). However, the ruling differs for mustawqa dirhams (those with severe adulteration) because the term 'dirham' does not include them. Thus, if the claim is made disconnectedly about mustawqa, it is not accepted. The commentator distinguishes between cases where the acknowledgment was of 'right' (including quality implicitly) versus 'good dirhams' (explicitly stating quality). In the latter, later claiming counterfeit is contradictory. The key principle is that the word 'dirham' encompasses various types, but excessive adulteration excludes a coin from the category.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Dirhams rejected by the treasury because they are counterfeit.
نبهرجة (nubahrja): Dirhams rejected by merchants, possibly minted outside the ruler's authority.
ستوقة (mustawqa): Dirhams with predominant adulteration, such that they are not considered dirhams.
موصول (muwasul): A statement where the acknowledgment and the subsequent claim are made in the same breath, without intervening separation.
مفصول (mafsul): A statement where the acknowledgment and the claim are made separately, with a clear break.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, if a person acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams as 'my right' (not specifying quality), then later in a separate conversation claims they were nubahrja, is he believed?
A) Yes, because nubahrja are covered by the term 'dirham'.
B) No, because the statement is disconnected.
C) Yes, but only if he had witnesses.
D) No, because nubahrja is not considered dirham.

Key Takeaway
The claim of counterfeit dirhams after acknowledgment of receipt is accepted with oath for zuyuf and nubahrja regardless of connection, but for mustawqa only if the claim is connected to the acknowledgment.

Seller's Denial of Sale and Claim of Acquittal from Defects
Ruling rejecting seller's evidence of acquittal after denying sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the seller's evidence of acquittal when he denies the sale, applying the principle of tanaqud (contradiction).

Core Idea
Denying the sale contradicts claiming an acquittal clause that only exists within a sale.

This case illustrates a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh: a person cannot take inconsistent positions in court. When a seller denies ever selling an item, he cannot then turn around and claim that the sale included a clause of acquittal from defects. The very existence of such a clause presupposes the sale. The Hanafi school rejects the seller's evidence due to this contradiction, ensuring consistency in legal proceedings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contradictory claims
The seller first denies the sale entirely (ma bi'tuha minka qatt). Later, he claims the buyer was acquitted from all defects (bara'a min kulli 'ayb). These two statements cannot both be true.
The denial contradicts the acquittal because the acquittal is part of a sale contract.
2
Recognize the nature of the acquittal clause
Stipulating acquittal (bara'a) is a modification of the contract, changing the default rule that the seller guarantees against hidden defects. It is a contractual term that only makes sense if a valid sale exists.
The acquittal clause is an attribute of the sale; an attribute cannot exist without its subject.
3
Apply the principle of tanaqud (contradiction)
The seller's evidence of acquittal is rejected because it is inherently contradictory to his earlier denial. Hanafi law does not allow a litigant to take inconsistent positions to gain advantage.
This principle upholds the integrity of judicial proceedings.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the seller's evidence (bayyinah) is not accepted (lam tuqbal). The buyer retains the right to return the defective item (khiyar al-'ayb).
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ادعى على آخر أمته، فقال: لم أبعها منك قط فبرهن على الشراء، فوجد بها عيبا، فبرهن البائع أنه برئ إليه من كل عيب، لم تقبل. لم تقبل: [بينة البائع على البراءة؛ للتناقض (ط، معدن)] أي إذا ادعى رجل على آخر بيع جاريته منه فقال المدعى عليه: لم أبعها منك قط، فأقام المدعي البينة على الشراء من البائع ثم وجد بها عيبا لا يحدث مثله في مثل تلك المدة كالإصبع الزائدة وأراد ردها على البائع فأثبت البائع بالبينة أن المشتري بريء إليه من كل عيب في الجارية لم تقبل بينة البائع؛ لأن اشتراط البراءة تغيير للعقد من اقتضاء وصف السلامة إلى غيره، فيقتضي وجود العقد؛ إذ الصفة بدون الموصوف لا يتصور، =
And whoever claims from another (the sale of) his slave-girl, and he (the seller) says: I did not sell her to you at all, then he (the buyer) proves the purchase, and finds a defect in her, then the seller proves that he (the buyer) is acquitted from every defect, it is not accepted. 'It is not accepted': [The seller's evidence of acquittal is not accepted due to contradiction]. That is, if a man claims from another the sale of his slave-girl from him, and the defendant says: I did not sell her to you at all, then the plaintiff produces evidence of the purchase from the seller, then finds a defect that does not usually occur in such a period, like an extra finger, and wants to return her to the seller, then the seller proves by evidence that the buyer is acquitted from every defect in the slave-girl, the seller's evidence is not accepted; because stipulating acquittal changes the contract from requiring the attribute of soundness to something else, so it necessitates the existence of the contract; since the attribute cannot be separated from the described.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a seller denies selling a slave-girl, but the buyer proves the sale and finds a pre-existing defect. When the seller tries to present evidence that the buyer was acquitted from all defects, that evidence is rejected. The reasoning is that the acquittal clause (stipulation of immunity) is a modification of the contract, implying the contract's existence. Since the seller denied the contract itself, the acquittal clause is contradictory. The commentator gives the example of an extra finger, a defect that would not occur after purchase. The key principle is that an attribute (defect exemption) cannot exist without its object (the contract).

Key Terms
براءة (bara'a): Acquittal from defects; a stipulation that the buyer waives the right to return due to defects.
عيب (ayb): A defect in the sold item that would give the buyer a right of return (khiyar al-'ayb).
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction: here, denying the sale while claiming an acquittal that only exists within a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the defect occurred after the sale.

Key Takeaway
The seller's evidence of acquittal is rejected because it contradicts his denial of the sale.

Ruling on 'In Sha Allah' in Documents: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Explains the effect of istithna on document validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn) on the effect of 'in sha Allah' at the end of a legal document and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Writing 'in sha Allah' in a legal document invalidates it; does it affect the entire document or only the last clause?

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of the phrase 'in sha Allah' when appended to a legal document. While all agree the document is invalidated to some extent, the key disagreement is whether the entire document and its underlying transaction are void, or only the last clause. We will explore the reasoning based on qiyas (Abu Hanifa) and istihsan (Sahibayn) and identify the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the point of contention? The effect of 'in sha Allah' at the end of a document.
The phrase acts as an exception (istithna) that invalidates what it attaches to.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds the entire document invalid; all clauses are conjoined so the exception applies to all.
He compares to a statement like 'your slave is free, your wife is divorced, and you must walk to the House of Allah if Allah wills' – the exception covers all.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only the last clause is invalid; the rest remains valid because clauses are independent and documents are written for continuity.
They use istihsan to preserve the transaction, as invalidating the entire document would contradict the parties' intent.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In a sale deed, Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale and deed; Sahibayn keeps the sale valid and only voids the last clause (e.g., guarantee of liability).
This affects the outcome of the transaction significantly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصّكُ بـ"إن شاء الله". ، ويبطل الصك إلخ هذا نصف بيت من أبيات المنظومة، وتمامه أي المصراع الثاني منه "وأبطلا آخره لا الجملة"، أي يبطل صك الشراء أو الإقرار إذا كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى، حتى يبطل الشراء والإقرار بذلك اتفاقاً، ولا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن الاستثناء مبطل على ما عرف في موضعه، ولو كتب في آخر الصك: ومن قام بهذا الحق، فهو وكيل إن شاء الله، أو كتب في صك الشراء: فما أدرك فلاناً من درك، فعلى فلان خلاصه وتسليمه إن شاء الله، بطل الصك كله عند أبي حنيفة، حتى يبطل الإقرار والشراء. وقالا : ينصرف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه وهو الأخير فيبطل به ضمان الدرك ،والتوكيل ويبقى الدين على حاله؛ إذ الأصل في الكلام الاستقلال في الجمل، والصك يكتب للاستئناف، فلو انصرف إلى الكل، كان مبطلاً له، فيكون ضد ما قصدوه، فينصرف إلى ما يليه ضرورة وله أن الكل لشيء واحد بحكم العطف، فينصرف إلى الكل كما في الكلمات المعطوفة بعضها على بعض مثل قوله : عبده حر، وامرأته طالق وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله إن شاء الله تعالى، أنه ينصرف إلى الجميع، وقول الإمام هو القياس، وقولهما هو الاستحسان. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٤/٢]
The document with 'if Allah wills'. And it invalidates the document etc. This is half a couplet from the verses of the poem, and its completion, i.e., the second hemistich of it: 'And it invalidates the end, not the entire sentence.' Meaning: The document of purchase or acknowledgment is invalidated if 'if Allah wills' is written at its end, to the extent that the purchase and acknowledgment are invalidated by that unanimously, and nothing is binding upon him; because the exception invalidates as established in its proper place. If he wrote at the end of the document: 'Whoever undertakes this right, he is an agent if Allah wills,' or wrote in the document of sale: 'What afflicts so-and-so from liability, then upon so-and-so is its release and delivery if Allah wills,' the entire document is invalidated according to Abu Hanifa, to the extent that the acknowledgment and purchase are invalidated. The two said: The exception refers to what is next to it, which is the last, so it invalidates the guarantee of liability and the agency, and the debt remains as it was; because the principle in speech is independence of clauses, and the document is written for continuity, so if it referred to all, it would invalidate it, which is contrary to what they intended, so it refers to what is next to it out of necessity. And his argument: That the whole is one thing by virtue of conjunction, so it refers to the whole, as in words conjoined to one another, such as his statement: 'His slave is free, and his wife is divorced, and upon him is walking to the House of Allah if Allah wills,' that it refers to all. The statement of the Imam is analogy, and their statement is juristic preference.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of writing 'إن شاء الله' (if Allah wills) at the end of a legal document. The source text is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, and the commentary expands on the ruling. The issue: if a document (such as a sale deed or acknowledgment) ends with the phrase 'in sha Allah', does this invalidate the entire document or only the last clause? All schools agree that the document is invalidated to some extent. However, Abu Hanifa holds that the entire document is invalidated, including the underlying transaction (sale or acknowledgment). His reasoning (qiyas) is that all clauses are conjoined, so the exception applies to all. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that only the last clause (e.g., the guarantee of liability or agency) is affected, and the main transaction remains valid. They base this on istihsan (juristic preference), considering the intent of the parties and the principle that each clause is independent. The passage concludes with a supporting example from conditional divorces and vows, where the exception applies to all clauses because they are conjoined. The commentary notes that Abu Hanifa's view is the qiyas (analogy), while the Sahibayn's view is istihsan.

Key Terms
صك (sakk): A legal document, such as a deed of sale or acknowledgment.
إن شاء الله (in sha Allah): A phrase meaning 'if Allah wills', used as a conditional exception in documents.
ضمان الدرك (daman al-darak): Guarantee against defects or eviction in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a sale deed ends with 'in sha Allah', what is the effect?
A) The entire deed and sale are invalid.
B) Only the last clause is invalid, sale remains valid.
C) The deed is valid if the seller intended it as a blessing.
D) The sale is valid but the deed is void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on the scope of istithna in documents: Abu Hanifa's qiyas voids the entire document, while Sahibayn's istihsan limits the effect to the last clause.

Ruling on Buyer's Recourse When Slave Reclaimed or Dies Before Delivery
Ruling on buyer's recourse when slave reclaimed or dies before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buyer's recourse against the guardian when the sold slave is reclaimed before delivery, and identify the guardian's recourse against the creditors and the dispute regarding recourse from newly discovered property.

Core Idea
The buyer recovers from the guardian, who recovers from the creditors; but recourse from newly discovered estate property is disputed.

When a judge orders a guardian to sell a slave from the deceased's estate to pay creditors, what happens if the slave is later reclaimed by a rightful owner or dies before delivery? Who bears the loss? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes a chain of recourse: the buyer sues the guardian, and the guardian sues the creditors. But an internal Hanafi dispute arises about whether the guardian can also recover from newly discovered property of the deceased. Understanding this ruling clarifies the liability of each party in estate sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Context
Recognise that the ruling concerns a sale by a guardian (wasi) on behalf of the creditors of the deceased, ordered by a judge. The slave is sold to satisfy debts.
The guardian may be appointed by the deceased or by the judge.
2
Determine the Buyer's Recourse When the Slave Is Reclaimed or Dies Before Delivery
If the slave is reclaimed (mustahaqq) by a third party or dies before the buyer takes delivery, and the price is lost, the buyer recovers the price from the guardian, not from the judge or the creditors directly.
The guardian is the contracting party; liability attaches to him.
3
Trace the Guardian's Recourse Against the Creditors
The guardian, after compensating the buyer, recovers the price from the creditors because he acted as their agent. The sale was for their benefit, so the liability ultimately falls on them.
The judge or his agent is not liable—they acted in an official capacity.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Recourse from Newly Discovered Property
If after the sale, new property of the deceased is found, the creditor can claim his debt from it. However, whether the guardian can also recover from that property for the loss he incurred is disputed: the preferred view (al-awwal asahh) allows it, but al-Sarakhsi denies it.
This dispute affects whether the guardian has a secondary source of recovery.
5
Recognize the Mufta Bihi and Authority
The relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that the buyer recovers from the guardian, and the guardian from the creditors. The dispute on recourse from newly discovered property remains unresolved for fatwa, but the matn prefers permissibility.
The tarjih authority is the matn's phrase 'al-awwal asahh'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(معدن)] ولو منصوب القاضي؛ لأنه نصبه؛ ليكون قائماً مقام الميت، فصار كمن أوصى إليه الميت أي إذا أمر القاضي الوصي ببيع العبد لأرباب الديون، فاستحق العبد أو مات قبل التسليم إلى المشتري، وضاع الثمن، رجع المشتري على الوصي؛ لأنه هو العاقد نيابة عن الميت، ويرجع الوصي على الغرماء؛ لأنه عامل لهم، ومن عمل لغيره عملاً، ولحقه بسببه ضمان، يرجع به على من يقع له العمل، ولو ظهر للميت بعد ذلك ،مال، رجع الغريم فيه بدينه؛ لأنه لم يصل إليه، وهل يرجع . بذلك أيضاً؛ لأن هذا الضمان لحقه في أمر الميت. في ذلك المال؟ قيل: يجوز أن وقيل: ليس له ذلك؛ لأنه إنما ضمن من حيث أن العقد وقع له فلم يكن له أن يرجع على غيره، والوارث إذا بيع له بمنزلة الغريم؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن في التركة دين، كان العاقد عاملاً له، والأول أصح، وصحح مجد الأئمة السرخسي عدم الرجوع، فقد اختلف التصحيح. (عيني، فتح) قبل القبض : أي قبل قبض المشتري من الوصي. (عيني) على الوصي بثمن العبد؛ لأنه هو العاقد نيابة عن الميت فيرجع الحقوق عليه كما إذا وكله حال حياته. (عيني)
And when the judge takes the money, and (the slave) is lost or reclaimed, he (the judge) does not guarantee, and the buyer recovers from the creditors. And if the judge or his agent (takes the price) and (the slave) is taken from the buyer's hand—meaning the buyer of the mentioned slave—and the judge ordered the guardian to sell it for them, and it was reclaimed or died before delivery, and the money was lost, the buyer recovers the price from the guardian, and he (the guardian) recovers from the creditors, meaning the guardian recovers because he acts for them. (Ayni) And when the judge orders: the restriction to the judge's order is agreed upon, and its ruling without his order is known by priority. The guardian: [whether he is the guardian of the deceased or the guardian appointed by the judge. (Ma'dan)] Even if appointed by the judge because he appointed him to stand in place of the deceased, so he becomes like one to whom the deceased appointed guardianship. That is, when the judge ordered the guardian to sell the slave to the creditors, and the slave was reclaimed or died before delivery to the buyer, and the price was lost, the buyer recovers from the guardian because he is the contractor on behalf of the deceased; and the guardian recovers from the creditors because he acts for them. And if someone works for another and due to that he incurs liability, he recovers it from the one for whom the work is done. And if later property of the deceased appears, the creditor recovers his debt from it because he did not receive it. And does he also recover from that (the property) because this liability he incurred for the deceased's matter? It is said: it is permissible that... and it is said: he does not have that because he was liable from the aspect that the contract was for him, so he could not recover from another. And the heir, if a sale is made for him, is like the creditor because if there is no debt in the estate, the contractor acts for him. And the first is more correct, and Majd al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi affirmed non-recourse, so there is disagreement on the correct position. (Ayni, Fath) Before delivery: meaning before the buyer takes possession from the guardian. (Ayni) On the guardian for the price of the slave because he is the contractor on behalf of the deceased, so rights are against him just as if the deceased had appointed him during his life. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a sale by a guardian (wasi) on behalf of creditors, with commentary from 'Ayni, Fath al-Qadir, and Ma'dan. The ruling states that if a judge orders a guardian to sell a slave belonging to the deceased's estate to satisfy debts, and the slave is later reclaimed (mustahaqq) by a rightful owner or dies before delivery to the buyer, and the price is lost, the buyer has recourse against the guardian for the price. The guardian, in turn, has recourse against the creditors because he acted as their agent. The commentary explains that the judge or his agent does not guarantee the price even if the slave is taken from the buyer's hand, because the judge acted in his official capacity. The guardian's liability arises from his role as the contracting party. The passage also discusses an ikhtilaf about whether the guardian can also recover from newly discovered property of the deceased; the preferred view (al-awlah asahh) is that he can, but al-Sarakhsi denies recourse. The commentary emphasizes that the ruling applies whether the guardian is appointed by the deceased or by the judge.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Guardian appointed by the deceased or by the judge to manage the estate.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors of the deceased estate.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): The claim of ownership by a third party that proves the slave was not property of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ruling, if the slave is reclaimed after sale by the guardian, who does the buyer recover the price from?
A) The judge
B) The guardian (wasi)
C) The creditors directly
D) The deceased's estate

Key Takeaway
The buyer recovers from the guardian, who recovers from the creditors; recourse from newly discovered property is disputed but the preferred view allows it.

Three Levels of Ambiguity in Agency for Purchase
Ruling on agency with ambiguity in the object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ambiguity in agency for purchase into slight, moderate, and gross, and state the ruling for each level.

Core Idea
Agency tolerates type ambiguity but not genus ambiguity; moderate ambiguity requires specifying price or type.

When you appoint someone to buy something for you, how specific must you be? The Hanafi school distinguishes between three levels of ambiguity. Slight ambiguity (type, like 'horse') is always valid; gross ambiguity (genus, like 'animal') invalidates the agency; moderate ambiguity (like 'slave') is valid only if price or type is specified. This lesson unpacks that taxonomy and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three ambiguities
Identify the three grades: jahalat al-naw' (type), jahalat al-jins (genus), and an intermediate level (e.g., 'slave'). Understand how each is defined.
Type: specific kind under a genus (e.g., horse). Genus: broad category covering multiple types (e.g., animal). Intermediate: a single type but lacks further specification (e.g., slave without origin).
2
Assign ruling to each
Apply the text's rule: slight ambiguity → valid even without price; gross ambiguity → invalid even with price; moderate → valid only if price or type is specified.
For moderate, if price is given, the type becomes known; if type is given (e.g., Turkish slave), price may be discovered customarily.
3
Note the exception for houses
The author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) treats a house as moderate (like a slave), but his own Fatawa treat it as slight (like a garment). This shows intra-madhhab variation.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the mufta bihi is the text's ruling, but the issue is debated.
4
Identify the proof
The proof for tolerating slight ambiguity is that agency is built on expansion (tawsi'ah) and is a request for help; requiring full detail would cause hardship.
This distinguishes agency from sale contracts, where gharar is stricter.

(i.e., the agency) (i.e., even if he did not name a price i.e., even if he ordered him to buy etc.) (i.e., not valid) (the agency is valid) (because this is ambiguity of genus) Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase: He presented from the chapters of agency what is most frequent and most needed, namely agency for sale and purchase, and he presented the discussion of purchase first because it indicates the establishment of ownership, while sale indicates its removal, and removal comes after establishment; or because purchase brings ownership of the sold item, and sale removes it, although it brings ownership of the price, and bringing the sold item is stronger than bringing the price because the basis in the chapter of sale is the sold item; or because purchase is realized with both existing and non-existing items, sometimes with existing (spot) and sometimes with non-existing (credit), unlike sale which is only realized with existing items because of the prohibition of selling non-existing items. Genus is that under which there are different types, and type is that which falls under a uniform name above it. Requiring specification of description involves some hardship, which is obviated. This applies whether he specified a price or not, because by specifying the genus the price becomes known customarily. There is a saying from al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad that this agency is not valid. Then if the agent buys at a price like its value or within the range of what people overlook, it is effective for the principal; if he buys with gross extravagance, it is effective for the agent, unlike the agent in sale, who may sell for little or much according to Imam.
