And if the agent buys, the purchase occurs for him. All that we have mentioned applies when there is no indication in the command of generality. If there is such indication, e.g., he says 'Buy for me whatever you see', then the agency is valid, and whatever he buys for him, he is complying thereby, and it occurs from the command. =
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on agency for sale and purchase. It begins by explaining why the discussion of purchase precedes sale: purchase establishes ownership while sale removes it, and establishment precedes removal. Additionally, purchase can be for both existing and non-existing items (spot and credit), whereas sale is limited to existing items due to the prohibition of selling non-existing items. The core ruling addresses the validity of agency when the object to be purchased is ambiguous. The text classifies ambiguity into three levels: slight (yasirah) or ambiguity of pure type (jins mahd), e.g., a horse – agency is valid even without specifying price; gross (fahishah) or ambiguity of genus (jins), e.g., an animal – agency is invalid regardless of price specification; and moderate (mutawassitah), e.g., a slave – valid only if the price or the type (e.g., Turkish) is specified. The proof for tolerating slight ambiguity is that agency is based on expansion (tawsi'ah) and is a request for help (istighathah), so requiring detailed description would cause hardship. The passage then notes an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that such an agency is not valid even with slight ambiguity; however, if the agent buys at a fair price, it binds the principal, but if he buys at an excessively high price, it binds the agent. This contrasts with the Hanafi position, where the agent in sale has more discretion. The passage concludes with a scenario where the principal's command indicates generality (e.g., 'buy whatever you see'), in which case the agency is valid regardless of ambiguity.

Key Terms
جهالة الجنس (jahalat al-jins): Ambiguity at the level of genus (jins), i.e., the category is too broad (e.g., 'animal' for a horse/camel), which invalidates the agency.
جهالة النوع (jahalat al-naw'): Ambiguity at the level of type (naw'), which is considered slight (yasirah) and is tolerated in agency.
توسعة (tawsi'ah): Expansion or leniency; the principle that agency is based on ease and cooperation, so minor ambiguities are overlooked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following agencies is valid without specifying a price?
A) Buy me an animal.
B) Buy me a garment (without specifying city).
C) Buy me a horse.
D) Buy me a slave.

Key Takeaway
Agency for purchase is invalid only when the object's genus is unknown; type ambiguity is tolerated due to the principle of expansion (tawsi'ah).

6 min
The Principle of Expansion and the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Dispute on validity of agency with ambiguity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for tolerating ambiguity in agency (tawsi'ah) and articulate the Shafi'i counter-argument, resulting in practical differences.

Core Idea
Agency is built on ease, so minor uncertainty is allowed; opponents require certainty to avoid gharar.

Why does the Hanafi school allow buying 'a horse' without specifying price, while others require full clarity? The key is the legal nature of agency: it is a contract of help (istighathah) based on expansion (tawsi'ah). This lesson explores the proof behind the Hanafi position and the opposing view of Imams al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and what this means in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school holds that slight ambiguity (type) does not invalidate agency because agency is built on expansion (tawsi'ah). The proof is that requiring full specification would cause hardship (haraj), which is removed in this contract.
The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and the general principle of ease.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that any ambiguity about the object makes the agency invalid, as the sale itself (which the agent will execute) must be free of gharar.
They analogize agency to the sale contract itself, where the object and price must be known.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, if the agent buys a vaguely described item (e.g., a horse) at a reasonable price, it binds the principal. Under Shafi'i, it does not bind the principal unless ratified; otherwise the agent is liable.
This is the practical difference: ownership and risk transfer.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is implicit: the need (hajah) for ease in agency outweighs the risk of gharar, especially since custom usually resolves the price determination.
The text does not refute the opponent directly but relies on the foundation of tawsi'ah.

('Ayni, Ma'din) Valid: i.e., the agency, so that the purchase occurs for the principal. ('Ayni) Whether he specified a price or not: The principle in this agency is that if the object is general, or known, or involves slight ambiguity (that of pure type, like a horse), the agency is valid even if he did not specify the price; if the ambiguity is gross (that of genus, like an animal), the agency is invalid whether he specified the price or not; if the ambiguity is moderate (like a slave), then if he specified the price or type (e.g., Turkish) it is valid, otherwise no. Genus is that under which there are different types, and type is that which falls under a uniform name above it. Now that you know this, if a man orders another to buy a Herawi garment or a horse or a mule, the order is valid and the agency is permissible because only ambiguity of description remains, and ambiguity of description is tolerated in agency because it is based on expansion, being a request for help. Requiring specification of description involves some hardship, which is obviated. This applies whether he specified a price or not, because by specifying the genus the price becomes known customarily. There is a saying from al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad that this agency is not valid. Then if the agent buys at a price like its value or within the range of what people overlook, it is effective for the principal; if he buys with gross extravagance, it is effective for the agent, unlike the agent in sale, who may sell for little or much according to Imam. ('Ayni, Fath) And to buy: i.e., even if he did not specify a price, because slight ambiguity does not harm. (T) (Ma'din: If he specified a price, because by mentioning the price the type becomes known, whether the price specifies a type or not. And if he specified the type like an Abyssinian or Roman slave, it is valid even if he did not specify the price. So if the author had said 'if he specified a price or specified a type' it would have been better. And the author made the house like the slave, contrary to what is in his Fatawa, where he made the house like the garment. ('Ayni, Fath: No, i.e., the agency is not valid even if he specified a price because this is ambiguity of genus, so it is not valid. He did not specify. And if the agent buys, the purchase occurs for him. All that we have mentioned applies when there is no indication in the command of generality. If there is such indication, e.g., he says 'Buy for me whatever you see', then the agency is valid, and whatever he buys for him, he is complying thereby, and it occurs from the command. =
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on agency for sale and purchase. It begins by explaining why the discussion of purchase precedes sale: purchase establishes ownership while sale removes it, and establishment precedes removal. Additionally, purchase can be for both existing and non-existing items (spot and credit), whereas sale is limited to existing items due to the prohibition of selling non-existing items. The core ruling addresses the validity of agency when the object to be purchased is ambiguous. The text classifies ambiguity into three levels: slight (yasirah) or ambiguity of pure type (jins mahd), e.g., a horse – agency is valid even without specifying price; gross (fahishah) or ambiguity of genus (jins), e.g., an animal – agency is invalid regardless of price specification; and moderate (mutawassitah), e.g., a slave – valid only if the price or the type (e.g., Turkish) is specified. The proof for tolerating slight ambiguity is that agency is based on expansion (tawsi'ah) and is a request for help (istighathah), so requiring detailed description would cause hardship. The passage then notes an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold that such an agency is not valid even with slight ambiguity; however, if the agent buys at a fair price, it binds the principal, but if he buys at an excessively high price, it binds the agent. This contrasts with the Hanafi position, where the agent in sale has more discretion. The passage concludes with a scenario where the principal's command indicates generality (e.g., 'buy whatever you see'), in which case the agency is valid regardless of ambiguity.

Key Terms
توسعة (tawsi'ah): Expansion or leniency; the principle that agency is based on ease and cooperation, so minor ambiguities are overlooked.
غبن فاحش (ghaban fahish): Gross extravagance in price, where the buyer pays far above the market value; such a purchase binds the agent, not the principal.
استغاثة (istighathah): A request for help; the text describes agency as a form of istighathah, highlighting its cooperative nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the agent buys a Herawi garment at double the market value under Shafi'i law?
A) The purchase binds the principal because the agency was valid.
B) The purchase binds the agent because the agency was invalid.
C) The purchase is void and both parties must return.
D) The seller can choose to enforce against either principal or agent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's toleration of slight ambiguity in agency rests on the principle of expansion (tawsi'ah), while opponents require certainty; the practical difference is who bears the contract when the price is unfair.

Agency Validity and Levels of Ignorance (Jahalah) in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on agency validity based on specification of genus and type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between slight, intermediate, and excessive ignorance in agency and state the Hanafi ruling for each case, including the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Agency is built on ease: ignorance of the attribute is tolerated, but ignorance of the genus invalidates.

Agency (wakalah) is a flexible contract built on mutual trust. When a principal instructs an agent to purchase a specific good, the level of detail in describing the good determines the validity of the agency. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a threefold classification of ignorance (jahalah) — slight, intermediate, and excessive — and shows how each affects the ruling. It also engages with the Shafi'i objection that price uncertainty invalidates the agency altogether. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rules in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases given in the text
The text begins by stating three scenarios: (1) buying a Herati cloth, horse, or mule; (2) buying a slave or house; (3) buying a cloth or an animal. Each has a different ruling depending on whether the price or type is specified. Identify the ruling for each case.
The first case is valid without price; the second requires price or type; the third is invalid even with price.
2
Map the cases to the jahalah classification
The text then explicitly classifies ignorance into three categories: slight (jahalah yasirah) — ignorance of the attribute only, as when the type is specified (e.g., horse); excessive (jahalah fahishah) — ignorance of the genus (e.g., animal); intermediate (jahalah mutawassitah) — genus known but type not (e.g., slave). Connect each earlier case to these categories.
Herati cloth, horse, mule = slight; slave = intermediate; cloth, animal = excessive.
3
Extract the Hanafi principle behind the ruling
The text states that agency is built on flexibility (tawsi'ah) and is a form of seeking help (istighathah). Requiring full description would cause hardship (haraj). Therefore, slight ignorance is tolerated. Identify how this principle justifies the validity when only the attribute is unknown.
The price becomes known by custom (ta'mul) when the type is specified.
4
Identify the opposing school's argument and the Hanafi response
The text mentions that Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Imam Ahmad hold that such agency is invalid because of ignorance of the price (gharar). The Hanafi response is that the price is not entirely unknown — custom determines a fair price, and gharar only invalidates when the subject matter is unknown, but here the subject (e.g., horse) is known.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): Hanafi – binding on principal if fair price; Shafi'i – agency invalid, purchase for agent's own account.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Now test your understanding: if a principal says 'Buy me a car' (no make/model), the genus 'car' is known but the type (e.g., Toyota) is not. This is intermediate ignorance, so the agency is valid only if price or type is specified. If he says 'Buy me an animal' (genus unknown), it is excessive and invalid.
The three categories apply consistently across all goods.

Source Text

باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء أي في بيان أحكامها
أمره بشراء ثوب هروي أو فرس أو بغل، صح، سمّى ثمناً أو لا، وبشراء عبد أو دار صح إن سمى ثمناً، وإلا لا، وبشراء ثوب أو دابة لا وإن سمى ثمناً . الأصل في هذه الوكالة أنها إن عمت أو علمت أو جهلت جهالة يسيرة وهي جهالة النوع المحض كفرس ،صحت وإن لم يبين الثمن، وإن فاحشة وهي جهالة الجنس كدابة بطلت، سواء بين الثمن أو لم يبين، وإن متوسطة كعبد، فإن بين الثمن أو النوع كتركي صحت، وإلا لا، والجنس ما يدخل تحته أنواع متغايرة والنوع ما يدخل تحت اسم منتظم فوقه، وإذا عرفت هذا، فلو أمر رجل رجلاً بشراء ثوب هروي أو فرس أو بغل صح الأمر، وجاز التوكيل؛ لأنه لم يبق إلا جهالة الصفة، وجهالة الصفة متحملة في الوكالة؛ لأن مبناها على التوسعة؛ لكونها استغاثة. وفي اشتراط بيان الوصف بعض الحرج، وهو مدفوع، وسواء في ذلك سمى ثمناً أو لم يسم؛ لأنه ببيان الجنس يصير الثمن معلوماً عادة، وفي قول من الشافعي ورواية عن أحمد: لا تصح هذه الوكالة، ثم إذا اشترى الوكيل بمثل قيمته، أو بما يتغابن الناس فيه، نفذ على الموكل وإن اشتراه بغين فاحش نفذ على الوكيل، بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع، حيث كان له البيع بما قل أو كثر عند الإمام.
Chapter on Agency for Sale and Purchase, i.e., explaining its rulings. He ordered him to purchase a Herati cloth, a horse, or a mule: it is valid, whether he specified a price or not. And for purchasing a slave or a house: it is valid if he specified a price, otherwise not. And for purchasing a cloth or an animal: it is not valid even if he specified a price. The principle in this agency is that if it is general, known, or the ignorance is slight — which is ignorance of the pure type, like a horse — it is valid even if he did not specify the price. If the ignorance is excessive — which is ignorance of the genus, like an animal — it is invalid, whether he specified the price or not. If it is intermediate, like a slave, then if he specified the price or the type (e.g., Turkish) it is valid, otherwise not. The genus is that which includes different types under it, and the type is that which falls under a uniform name above it. Once you understand this, if a man ordered another to purchase a Herati cloth, a horse, or a mule, the order is valid and the agency is permissible, because only ignorance of the attribute remains, and ignorance of the attribute is tolerated in agency because its basis is flexibility, being a form of seeking help. Requiring specification of the attribute would cause hardship, which is to be avoided. This applies equally whether he specified a price or not, because by specifying the genus the price becomes known by custom. There is a saying of al-Shafi'i and a narration from Ahmad that this agency is not valid. Then if the agent purchases at a price similar to its value or at a price within the range of mutual concession, it is binding on the principal. If he purchases it with excessive overpayment, it is binding on the agent, contrary to the agent for sale, who, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, may sell for little or much.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the Hanafi ruling on the validity of agency (wakalah) for purchasing goods, distinguishing between levels of ignorance (jahalah) in specifying what is to be bought. The text first gives three categories: (1) specification by type (naw') such as a Herati cloth or a horse — here the agency is valid whether or not a price is named, because only the attribute (sifah) is unknown, and ignorance of the attribute is tolerated due to the flexible nature of agency. (2) Specification by genus (jins) such as a cloth or an animal — the agency is invalid even if a price is named, because ignorance of the genus is excessive and prevents the agent from knowing what to buy. (3) An intermediate case like a slave — valid if the price or a subtype (e.g., Turkish) is specified, otherwise invalid. The author then explains that genus includes multiple types (e.g., 'animal' includes horse, donkey, etc.), while type is a subcategory under a genus. The ruling is justified by the principle that agency is built on ease (tawsi'ah) and is a form of seeking help (istighathah), so specifying every attribute would cause undue hardship (haraj). The passage also includes an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Imam Ahmad hold that such an agency is invalid altogether. The Hanafi response is that the custom (ta'mul) makes the price known when the type is specified, and the flexibility in agency outweighs the need for exact specification. Finally, the text adds a detail: if the agent purchases at a fair price (misl al-qimah or within the range of mutual concession, taghabun), the purchase is binding on the principal; if he overpays excessively (ghabn fahish), it is binding on the agent. This differs from an agent for sale, who according to Abu Hanifa may sell at any price, little or much.

Key Terms
جهالة اليسيرة (jahālah yasīrah): Slight ignorance: ignorance of the specific attribute (sifah) while the type (naw') is known, e.g., a horse without specifying its color or breed.
جهالة الفاحشة (jahālah fāḥishah): Excessive ignorance: ignorance of the genus (jins), e.g., 'animal' without specifying whether it is a horse, donkey, etc. This invalidates the agency.
جهالة المتوسطة (jahālah mutawassiṭah): Intermediate ignorance: when the genus is known but the type is not, e.g., 'slave' without specifying Turkish, Indian, etc. Valid only if price or type is specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a principal tells his agent 'Buy me a horse' without mentioning the price, what is the ruling?
A) The agency is invalid because the price is unknown.
B) The agency is valid and the purchase is binding on the principal if bought at a fair price.
C) The agency is valid only if the agent pays from his own pocket.
D) The agency is invalid unless the type of horse is specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, agency for purchase is valid when the type is known, even without specifying the price, due to the flexible nature of agency and reliance on custom.

The Scope of 'Ta'am' in Agency for Purchase
Defining the default meaning of 'food' in a purchase agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default definition of 'ta'am' in purchase agency, identify the role of custom ('urf), and apply the ruling to scenarios involving custom and payment.

Core Idea
In Hanafi agency, the term 'food' defaults to wheat and flour, but custom can override this default.

When someone appoints an agent to buy 'food' without further specification, what exactly does 'food' mean? This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq unpacks the Hanafi default ruling and shows how custom, the amount of money, and contextual clues can shift the meaning. Understanding this is crucial for valid agency contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Ruling
Read the matn: 'wa bi-shira'i ta'amin yaqa'u 'ala al-barri wa daqiqihi.' State the default definition of 'food' in agency according to the Hanafi school.
The default is wheat (burr) and its flour (daqeeq), not all edibles.
2
Understand the Qiyas Objection
The text notes that by qiyas, 'food' should include all eatables like meat. Recognize that the default is an exception based on customary usage, not strict analogy.
The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held the default.
3
Note the Role of Custom ('Urf)
Later scholars introduced custom: Transoxianian custom includes cooked food; Sadr al-Shahid's fatwa relies on local custom. Understand that custom can override the default.
The mufta bihi position is that custom ('urf) determines the meaning.
4
Apply Quantitative Indicators
The amount of dirhams given by the principal signals intent: many dirhams indicate wheat, few indicate bread, middle amounts indicate flour. Practice mapping dirham ranges to meanings.
Example: 1-3 dirhams = few; 4-7 = middle; more = many.
5
Check Validity Conditions
The agency is valid only if the principal either specifies the quantity of food or pays the price. If not, the agency is invalid due to uncertainty (jahl) in quantity, which is like uncertainty in genus.
The agent must know what to buy to bind the principal.

وبشراء طعام يقع على البر ودقيقه. وبشراء طعام أي ولو أمر رجل رجلا بشراء إلخ. (عيني) يقع على البر ودقيقه حتى لا يكون له أن يشتري غيرهما من الطعام. (عيني) إن بين قدره أو دفع ثمنه، وإلا لم يجز على الآمر، والقياس أن يتناول كل مطعوم؛ اسم المهيأ للأكل كاللحم يعني لأنه له، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وقال بعض مشايخ ما وراء النهر : الطعام في عرفنا ينصرف إلى ما يمكن أكله، المطبوخ والمشوي وقال صدر الشهيد وعليه الفتوى وقيل إن كثرت الدراهم فعلى البر ، وإن قلت فعلى الخبز، وإن كانت بين الأمرين فعلى الدقيق والقلة مثل أن يدفع إليه درهما إلى ثلاثة والوسط مثل أربعة إلى سبعة. والطعام في عرف القاهرة: الطبيخ بالمرق واللحم، وإذا عرف بالاجتهاد أنه بالكثير من الدراهم يريد الخبز بأن كان عنده وليمة جاز له أن يشتري الخبز، وفي متخذ الوليمة يقع على الخبز بكل حال قلت الدراهم أو كثرت؛ لدلالة الحال، وإذا لم يدفع إليه الدراهم، وقال: اشتر لي طعاماً لم يجز على الآمر؛ لأنه وكله أن يشتري له مكيلاً، و لم يبين له ،مقداره وجهالة القدر في المكيلات والموزونات كجهالة الجنس من حيث إن الوكيل لا يقدر على تحصيل مقصود الآمر بما سمى له [رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۰/۲] (فتح)
And with the purchase of food, it applies to wheat and its flour. And with the purchase of food—meaning: if a man commands a man to purchase, etc. (Ayni) It applies to wheat and its flour, so that he may not buy other than those two from food. (Ayni) If he specified its quantity or paid its price, otherwise it is not valid on the commander. By analogy (qiyas), it should include every edible thing, which is a name for what is prepared for eating, like meat. Meaning: because it is for him, and the Three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said it. Some scholars of Transoxiana said: food in our custom refers to what can be eaten, cooked and grilled. Sadr al-Shahid said, and the fatwa is upon it, and it is said: if the dirhams are many, then it applies to wheat; if few, then to bread; if between the two, then to flour and unhusked grain. For example, he gives him one to three dirhams, and the middle is like four to seven. And food in the custom of Cairo is stew with broth and meat. And if it is known by ijtihad that with many dirhams he intends bread, such as when he has a wedding feast, then it is permissible for him to buy bread. And in the case of one undertaking a wedding feast, it applies to bread in all cases, whether the dirhams are few or many, due to the indication of the situation. And if he did not give him the dirhams and said: 'Buy food for me,' it is not valid on the commander, because he appointed him to buy a measured thing for him and did not specify its quantity, and ignorance of quantity in measured and weighed things is like ignorance of genus, in that the agent is unable to achieve the commander's intent by what he named. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 170/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the term 'ta'am' (food) when a principal appoints an agent to buy food without specification. The default ruling in the Hanafi school, as stated in the original text, is that 'food' refers specifically to wheat (burr) and its flour (daqeeq). The commentary expands on this, noting that by strict analogy (qiyas), 'food' would include all edible items, but the school restricts it to wheat and flour based on customary usage ('urf). The text then presents a difference among later Hanafi scholars: those from Transoxiana consider cooked and grilled items as food, while Sadr al-Shahid's view, which is relied upon for fatwa, gives weight to local custom. The amount of money given also influences interpretation: many dirhams indicate wheat, few indicate bread, and a middle amount indicates flour. In Cairo, 'food' means stew with broth and meat. If the context of a wedding feast indicates he wants bread, the agent may buy bread. Importantly, if no dirhams are handed over and no quantity specified, the agency is invalid due to uncertainty (jahl) in quantity, which is treated like uncertainty in genus. This passage teaches students that in Hanafi fiqh, custom ('urf) plays a significant role in determining the scope of ambiguous terms in contracts, and that the agent must adhere to the understood meaning to bind the principal.

Key Terms
طعام (ta'am): In this context, 'food' refers primarily to wheat (burr) and flour (daqeeq), not all edible items, although custom may expand it.
بر (burr): Wheat, the default meaning of 'food' in this agency context.
دقيق (daqeeq): Flour, specifically from wheat, included in the default meaning of 'food'.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice that can override the default meaning of a term in agency contracts.

C) The agency is invalid due to uncertainty in quantity.
D) The agency is valid regardless because the agent can use custom.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, the term 'food' defaults to wheat and flour, but custom, dirham amount, and context can modify its meaning; without specifying quantity or paying, the agency is invalid.

The Agent’s Right to Return Defective Goods
Explaining the agent's authority to reject defective purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that an agent may reject a defective purchase without the principal’s permission because it is a right of the contract, and apply this rule to scenarios involving agent’s liability and the principal’s role.

Core Idea
An agent has inherent authority to reject defective goods because the right to reject belongs to the contract, not the principal personally.

When an agent purchases goods for a principal, the agent is not merely a messenger but stands in the place of the principal for contractual rights. One of those rights is the option to return an item due to a defect. This lesson explains the scope of that authority, its transfer upon the agent’s death, and the nuances when the principal admits or denies the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is an agent returning a defective item to the seller without the principal’s permission. The ruling is that this is valid (sahih) and binding.
This ruling is extracted directly from the opening sentence: “Even without the permission of the principal, because it is among the rights of the contract.”
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a rational one (dalil aqli): the text states it is a right of the contract, and all rights of the contract are delegated to the agent. This is based on the nature of agency.
No explicit Quran or hadith is cited; the proof is derived from the legal maxim that an agent stands in the place of the principal for contractual matters.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase “min huquq al-aqd” (from the rights of the contract) indicates that the option to reject for defect is a contractual right, not a personal right of the principal. The agent, as the direct party to the contract, inherits these rights.
This reasoning distinguishes between rights that arise from the contract itself (which the agent may exercise) and rights that belong to the principal personally (which require permission).
4
Recognize exceptions and nuances
The authority ends after the agent delivers the goods to the principal, because the agency ends. Also, if the agent is under interdiction (mahjur), the rejection is done by the principal. Another nuance: if the principal admits a defect but the agent denies, nothing is binding; but if the agent admits and the principal denies, the agent is bound unless the defect could not have arisen later.
These exceptions show that the agent’s authority is not absolute and is bounded by the duration and capacity of agency.
5
Note the practical implications
If the agent rejects the defect, the item perishes from the principal’s wealth before rejection becomes binding on the agent. If the agent accepts the defect, it becomes binding on him, and the principal may choose to accept or hold the agent liable.
The ruling also applies to the agent’s heir or executor after the agent’s death, showing continuity of contractual rights.

الرد بالعيب إلخ: ولو بغير إذن الموكل؛ لأنه من حقوق العقد، وكلها إلى الوكيل، ولوارثه أو وصيه بعد موته، فإن لم يكونا فللموكل، وكذا الوكيل بالبيع ولو رضي الوكيل بالعيب لزمه، ثم الموكل إن شاء قبله وإن شاء لزم الوكيل، وقبل أن يلزم الوكيل لو هلك يهلك من مال الموكل، وأشار المصنف إلى أن الرد عليه لو كان وكيلا بالبيع فوجد المشتري بالمبيع عيبا ما دام الوكيل حياً عاقلا من أهل لزوم العهدة، وإن كان محجورا يرد على الموكل، وإلى أن الموكل أجنبي في الخصومة بالعيب، فلو أقر به الموكل وأنكره الوكيل لم يلزمهما شيء، بخلاف عكسه؛ فإنه يلزم الوكيل لا الموكل إلا أن يكون عيبا لا يحدث مثله في تلك المدة، للقطع بقيام العيب عند الموكل إلا بأمره لأنه انتهى حكم الوكالة بالتسليم إليه، ولأن فيه إبطال يده الحقيقية فلا يتمكن منه إلا بإذنه، وقيد بالعيب؛ لأنه لو وكله وسلمه وقبض الثمن وسلمه إلى الموكل؛ فله أن يفسخ ببيع متاعه فباع فاسداً البيع ويسترد الثمن من الموكل بغير رضاه؛ لحق الشرع. (فتح)
The rejection due to defect, etc.: Even without the permission of the principal, because it is among the rights of the contract, and all of them are delegated to the agent, and to his heir or executor after his death; if they are not present, then to the principal. Similarly, the agent for sale: if the agent accepts the defect, it is binding on him; then the principal, if he wishes, may accept it, and if he wishes, it is binding on the agent. And before it becomes binding on the agent, if it perishes, it perishes from the wealth of the principal. And the author indicated that the rejection (radd) is upon him if he was an agent for sale and the buyer finds a defect in the sold item as long as the agent is alive and sane, among those capable of assuming liability; and if he is under interdiction (mahjur), the rejection is upon the principal. And that the principal is a stranger in the litigation over the defect. So if the principal admits the defect and the agent denies it, nothing is binding on them, contrary to the opposite, for it is binding on the agent, not the principal, unless it is a defect that does not usually occur in that period, due to certainty that the defect existed with the principal. Except with his permission, because the authority of agency ended with delivery to him, and because in it is cancellation of his actual possession, so he cannot do it except with his permission. And it is restricted to defect because if he delegated him and he delivered and took the price and delivered it to the principal, he may rescind if he sold his goods and the sale was invalid, and he reclaims the price from the principal without his consent, due to the right of Shariah.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from Fath, discusses the agent's right to return a defective item (al-radd bi al-ayb). The ruling is that the agent has the authority to reject a purchase due to defect even without the principal's explicit permission. This is because the right to reject is one of the rights of the contract (huquq al-aqd), and all such rights are delegated to the agent as the direct contracting party. After the agent's death, this right passes to his heir or executor; if neither exists, it returns to the principal. The same applies to an agent for sale: if a buyer finds a defect, the agent handles the rejection while alive and sane. If the agent is under guardianship (mahjur), the rejection must be done by the principal. The passage distinguishes between cases where the principal admits the defect but the agent denies it—in which case neither is bound—versus the opposite (agent admits, principal denies), which binds the agent unless the defect could not have arisen later, indicating it existed with the principal. Once the agent has delivered the item to the principal, his agency ends, and he cannot reject without permission, as it would invalidate the principal's actual possession. This rule highlights that the agent acts as the principal's representative in contractual remedies.

Key Terms
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bi al-ayb): The legal right of an agent to return a purchased item due to a defect, without needing the principal’s permission.
حقوق العقد (huquq al-aqd): Rights arising from the contract itself, such as the option to reject for defect, which are delegated to the agent.
عهدة ('uhdah): Liability or responsibility, specifically the guarantee of soundness in a sale; the agent is subject to it while alive and sane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can an agent return a defective item to the seller without obtaining the principal's explicit permission, according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, the agent must always seek permission.
B) Yes, because the right to reject for defect is a right of the contract delegated to the agent.
C) Yes, but only if the defect is obvious.
D) No, because only the principal has standing in such matters.

Key Takeaway
The agent’s right to reject defects is a right of the contract, not a personal right of the principal, so the agent may exercise it without permission.

The Agent's Right to Detain: Intra-Hanafi Analysis of Liability After Destruction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on detention consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf/Muhammad vs. Zufar) regarding the agent's detention and apply the correct analogy to calculate the financial outcome when the item perishes.

Core Idea
After detention, the item is treated as a constructive sale—destruction forfeits the price, but Abu Yusuf likens it to a pawn, and Zufar to usurpation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines the agent's right to detain the bought item until payment. While all Hanafi imams agree on the basic right, they differ on what happens if the item is destroyed after detention. The debate hinges on which analogy governs: sale, pawn, or usurpation—each leading to different financial adjustments. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze legal analogies and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the common ground
Identify the agreed ruling: the agent has the right to detain the purchased item for prompt payment, as he is like a seller.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah; the dispute is about post-detention destruction.
2
Identify the triggering event
Focus on the scenario where the item perishes after the agent has taken lawful possession by way of detention.
Before detention, destruction is on the principal's account.
3
Outline the three analogical frames
List the three analogies: (1) Sale – the agent is a constructive seller (Abu Hanifa, majority); (2) Pawn – the item becomes security (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad); (3) Usurpation – detention is transgressive (Zufar).
Each analogy leads to different liability.
4
Work through numerical examples
Apply each analogy to scenarios where price and value differ. For sale: destruction cancels both. For pawn: set-off and balance. For usurpation: agent guarantees full value.
See text examples: price=15, value=10; price=10, value=15; equal values.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
From the commentators (Ayni, Fath), the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (pawn) is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This reflects a balanced approach, not the extreme of seller or usurper.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحبس المبيع: أي ويجوز أيضاً للوكيل حبس المبيع عن الموكل لاستيفاء الثمن؛ لأنه كالبائع، وهذا إذا كان الثمن حالاً، فإن اشتراه بثمن مؤجل تأجل في حق الموكل أيضاً، بخلاف ما إذا اشتراه بنقد ثمن، ثم أجله البائع كان للوكيل أن يطالبه به حالاً. لثمن دفعه أي لأجل استيفاء ثمن من ماله، فلو هلك في يده قبل حبسه هلك من مال الموكل، ولم يسقط الثمن، وإن هلك بعد حبسه فهو كالمبيع من الموكل حبس لثمن دفعه هذا ليس بقيد لازم بل اتفاقي؛ لأنه لو لم يدفع الثمن له أن يحبسه عنه أيضاً، وقال زفر: ليس للوكيل ذلك؛ لأن قبضه كقبضه، فكأنه سلمه إليه، فيسقط الحبس وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا: أنه كالبائع من الموكل فيثبت له حق الحبس [رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۰/۲] مال الموكل لأن يد الوكيل كيد الموكل. ولم يسقط الثمن أي عن الموكل فيرجع الوكيل عليه؛ لأن يده كيده؛ إذ الوكيل عامل له، فإذا لم يحبس يصير الموكل قابضاً بقبضه حكماً. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۱/۲] (فتح) وإن هلك قيد بالهلاك؛ لأنه لو ذهبت عينه عنده بعد حبسه لم يسقط شيء من الثمن؛ لأنه وصف، والأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء، لكن يخير الموكل إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء تركه، وقوله: "فهو كالمبيع" أي عند الطرفين؛ لأن الوكيل بمنزلة البائع من الموكل وكان حبسه لأجل استيفاء الثمن، فيسقط الثمن بهلاكه فيهلك بالثمن، قلت قيمته أو كثرت، فلا رجوع لأحدهما على الآخر بشيء، وعند أبي يوسف هو كهلاك الرهن، لأنه صار مضموناً بالحبس للاستيفاء بعد أن لم يكن مضموناً به، وهذا هو معنى الرهن، بخلاف المبيع؛ فإنه مضمون بنفس العقد حبسه البائع أو لم يحبسه، ولهما: أن بينهما مبادلة حكمية بدليل ما ذكرنا من الأحكام فيكون معتبراً بالمبادلة الحقيقية، وهو البيع. كالرهن وقال زفر هو كهلاك المغصوب فعليه ضمان مثله إن كان مثلياً، وضمان قيمته إن كان قيمياً؛ لأنه ليس له الحبس عنده، فالحبس يكون متعدياً، فيكون كالغصب وبه قالت الثلاثة، وتوضيحه إذا كان الثمن خمسة عشر مثلاً، وقيمة المبيع عشرة يرجع الوكيل بخمسته على الموكل ويتقاصان العشرة بالعشرة عند أبي يوسف بناء على ضمان الرهن، ويرجع الموكل على الوكيل بمثل المبيع، أو بجميع قيمته عند زفر بناء على ضمان الغصب، وإذا كان الثمن عشرة، وقيمة المبيع خمسة عشر يتقاصان العشرة بالعشرة والخمسة أمانة في يد الوكيل عند من يقول بضمان الرهن، ويرجع الموكل على الوكيل بمثله إن كان مثلياً، أو بخمسة عشر عند من يقول بضمان الغصب، وإذا كان الثمن والقيمة على السواء يرجع الموكل بمثله على الوكيل فيطالبه الوكيل بالثمن، وإن لم يكن مثلياً يتقاصان، وعند من يقول بضمان المبيع يسقط الثمن كله فلا يجب عليه شيء. (عيني، فتح)
And the detention of the sold item: i.e., it is also permissible for the agent to detain the sold item from the principal in order to collect the price, because he is like a seller. This is if the price is prompt. If he bought it with a deferred price, it is deferred in the right of the principal as well, unlike if he bought it with cash, then the seller deferred it, then the agent may demand it promptly from the principal. For the price he paid—i.e., for the sake of collecting the price from his own wealth. So if it perishes in his hand before his detention, it perishes from the principal's wealth, and the price does not drop. If it perishes after his detention, it is like the sold item from the principal detained for the price he paid. This is not a necessary condition but incidental; because if he did not pay the price, he may also detain it from him. Zufar said: The agent does not have that right, because his taking possession is like his (the principal's) taking possession, as if he has delivered it to him, so the detention falls. And the Three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) said this. Our evidence: he is like a seller from the principal, so the right of detention is established for him. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 170/2] Wealth of the principal, because the agent's hand is like the principal's hand. And the price does not drop—i.e., from the principal, so the agent may claim it back from him, because his hand is like his hand; the agent is his worker. So if he does not detain, the principal becomes a possessor by his (the agent's) possession constructively. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 171/2] (Fath) And if it perishes—the condition of perishing is specified because if its essence goes missing after his detention, nothing of the price drops, because it is a description, and descriptions are not compensated by anything, but the principal may choose: if he wishes, he may take it with the full price, and if he wishes, he may leave it. And his saying: 'it is like the sold item'—i.e., from both sides, because the agent is in the position of a seller from the principal, and his detention was for the sake of collecting the price, so the price drops by its destruction, so it perishes along with the price, whether its value is little or much, and neither has any recourse against the other in anything. According to Abu Yusuf, its destruction is like the destruction of a pawn (rahn), because it became subject to liability due to detention for collection after it was not subject to liability thereby, and that is the meaning of pawn. Unlike the sold item, which is subject to liability by the contract itself, whether the seller detains it or not. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), there is a constructive exchange between them, based on what we mentioned of rulings, so it is considered like real exchange, which is sale. Like a pawn. Zufar said: it is like the destruction of a usurped item, so he must guarantee its equivalent if it is a fungible, and its value if it is a non-fungible, because he does not have the right of detention with him, so the detention is transgressive, and it becomes like usurpation. And the Three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said this. Clarification: if the price is, for example, fifteen, and the value of the sold item is ten, the agent recovers his five from the principal, and they set off the ten against the ten according to Abu Yusuf based on the guarantee of pawn. And the principal recovers from the agent the equivalent of the sold item, or all its value according to Zufar based on the guarantee of usurpation. If the price is ten and the value of the sold item is fifteen, they set off ten against ten, and the five is a trust in the agent's hand according to those who say the guarantee of pawn, and the principal recovers from the agent its equivalent if it is a fungible, or fifteen according to those who say the guarantee of usurpation. If the price and value are equal, the principal recovers its equivalent from the agent, so the agent demands the price from him, and if it is not a fungible, they set off. According to those who say the guarantee of the sold item, the price all drops, so nothing is obligatory on him. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from Fath and Ayni, addresses the agent's right to detain the purchased item (habs al-mabi') until the principal pays the price. The agent is likened to a seller, and thus has the same right of detention (habs). This applies when the price is prompt; if the price was deferred, it remains deferred for the principal as well. The passage then explores the intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Yusuf/Muhammad vs. Zufar) regarding the effect of destruction after detention. The majority (al-thalatha: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that after detention, the item is considered like a sale between the agent and principal: if it perishes, the price is forfeited, and no further claim exists. Abu Yusuf analogizes it to a pawn (rahn): the item becomes a security, so if it perishes, the price drops only to the extent of its value, with possible set-offs. Zufar analogizes it to usurpation (ghasb): since he does not have the right to detain, it's a wrongful act, so the agent must guarantee the item fully. The passage provides detailed numerical examples to illustrate the differences in financial consequences when the price and value differ. This lesson demonstrates how Hanafi imams may differ in applying analogies (qiyas) to new situations, and how the final fatwa (mufta bihi) is determined.

Key Terms
حبس المبيع (habs al-mabi'): The agent's right to withhold the purchased item from the principal until the price is paid, based on analogy to a seller's right.
الثلاثة (al-thalatha): Refers to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) plus either Abu Hanifa or Zufar depending on context; here it indicates the majority of Hanafi imams.
ضمان الرهن (daman al-rahn): Guarantee like that of a pawn: the detained item is a security for the debt, and loss reduces the debt proportionally.
ضمان الغصب (daman al-ghasb): Guarantee like that of usurpation: wrongful detention requires full compensation of the item's value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, after an agent detains a purchased item for the price, if the item is destroyed in the agent's possession, what is the ruling according to the majority view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad)?
A) The agent must pay the full value to the principal, like a usurper.
B) The price is forfeited, and the agent owes nothing further.
C) The principal may choose to take the price or demand the item's equivalent.
D) The item becomes a pawn, and only the difference is owed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers three analogies for the agent's detention; the relied-upon view treats the detained item like a pawn, requiring proportional adjustment when the item perishes.

Agency in Sarf and Salam: Possession and Separation
Ruling on the effect of agent's separation before possession in sarf and salam contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency in sarf and salam contracts and apply the condition that separation before possession invalidates the contract.

Core Idea
An agent's separation before possession voids the contract in sarf and salam.

This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanded by Imam Ayni, clarifies a critical condition in currency exchange (sarf) and forward sale (salam) when conducted through an agent. The agent, as the direct contracting party, must personally take possession of the counter-values and then separate; otherwise, the contract is invalid. The commentary also addresses a key exception: when the party receiving the price in salam appoints an agent, that agency is not valid because the agent would become the seller.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Core Ruling
Identify the two outcomes based on the agent's action: if the agent takes possession, the contract is completed; if he separates before possession, the contract is invalidated. This is the central legal rule for agency in sarf and salam.
The ruling hinges on the condition of possession (qabd) and separation (ifitiraq) being done in the correct order.
2
Understand the Rationale (Talil)
Note the reasoning: agency is permissible because the principal can perform the contract himself. However, the agent's role is critical because the contract's validity depends on the direct exchange of possession and separation without delay.
The contract type requires hand-to-hand exchange; the agent stands in the principal's place for this requirement.
3
Identify the Exception
Observe the exception: if the muslam ilayhi (the seller in salam) appoints an agent to receive the capital, the agency is not valid. This is because the agent would then become the seller, and the subject matter (muslam fihi) remains in the agent's liability, changing the contract's nature.
This exception prevents the sale from becoming a personal obligation of the agent.
4
Apply the Rule to a Scenario
Work through a practical example: Buyer appoints agent to conduct a sarf exchange. Agent takes the currency but leaves before the seller's currency is handed over. Ruling: contract invalid. Then check the exception: Seller appoints agent to receive payment in salam. Ruling: agency invalid.
Practice reinforces the condition and exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) أي إذا وكل شخصاً بأن يعقد عقد الصرف أو السلم في مكيل مثلاً، ففعل، جاز؛ لأنه عقد يملكه الموكل بنفسه، فيجوز التوكيل به، والمراد بالسلم الإسلام، وهو أن يوكل رجلاً ليسلم دراهم معدودة في كر معلوم، أما لو وكل مسلم إليه رجلاً بقبول الثمن، فإنه لا يجوز توكيله؛ لأن الوكيل إذا قبض رأس المال، يبقى المسلم فيه في ذمة الوكيل، وهو مبيع، =
The agent's separation in currency exchange (sarf) and forward sale (salam): meaning that he is the contractor, so if he takes possession, the contract is completed, and if he separates before possession, the contract is invalidated. (Ayni) That is, if a person appoints someone to contract a currency exchange or forward sale in a measured thing, for example, and he does so, it is permissible, because it is a contract that the principal can conclude himself, so agency for it is permissible. The meaning of salam is al-Islam, which is that he appoints a man to deliver counted dirhams for a known kurr (measure). However, if the one to whom the salam is made (muslam ilayhi) appoints a man to receive the price, then agency for it is not permissible, because if the agent receives the capital, the subject matter of the salam remains in the agent's liability, and it is a sold item, =
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from Ayni, addresses the specific ruling on the agent's separation in contracts of currency exchange (sarf) and forward sale (salam). The key principle is that the agent is the contracting party, and the contract's validity depends on possession and separation. In sarf and salam, it is essential that the parties separate after the contract while still in possession of the counter-values? Actually the text states: 'if he takes possession, the contract is completed, and if he separates before possession, the contract is invalidated.' This is a condition unique to sarf and salam, where hand-to-hand exchange is required (in sarf, both currencies must be exchanged on the spot; in salam, the price must be paid immediately). The agent, as the direct party, must effect this. The commentary explains that agency for such contracts is valid because the principal could do them himself. However, if the party who receives the price in salam (muslam ilayhi) appoints an agent to receive the capital, that agency is not valid, because if the agent receives the capital, the subject matter (muslam fihi) remains in the agent's liability, making him the seller, which changes the contract. The passage is incomplete, but it clarifies that the agent's personal action is crucial for these contracts.

Key Terms
مفارقة الوكيل (mufaraqat al-wakeel): The agent's physical separation from the other party after the contract, which is a requirement for validity in sarf and salam.
الصرف (al-sarf): Currency exchange, a contract requiring immediate possession and separation.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for future delivery of a specified commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The contract is valid because the agent took possession.

D) The contract is valid if the agent later returns.

Key Takeaway
In sarf and salam conducted through an agent, the contract is only valid if the agent takes possession before separating; otherwise, it is void.

Agent Buying Excess: The Hanafi Dispute on Ownership of Overpurchase
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on agent buying extra weighed goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys more than instructed for a weighed item, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An agent's excess purchase of weighed goods splits ownership: principal owns the specified quantity, agent owns the rest — according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage addresses a common scenario in agency: the agent exceeds the specified quantity. The Hanafi school disagrees on whether the excess belongs to the principal or the agent. The ruling depends on the type of goods (weighed vs unique) and the extent of the excess. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply agency law in trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
An agent is told to buy 10 ratls of meat for 1 dirham. He buys 20 ratls of the same quality. The dispute is whether the principal is obliged for 10 or 20 ratls.
The goods are mawzun (weighed fungible).
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and proof
Abu Hanifa: principal bound for 10 ratls at half the price (0.5 dirham). Proof: the agent exceeded the limited authorization; the excess is a violation, so it returns to the agent.
Excess is not authorized.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: principal bound for all 20 ratls at full price (1 dirham). Proof: the agent acted beneficially by adding good, similar to an agent selling a slave for more than instructed.
They analogize to selling at a higher price.
4
Analyse the conditions
The ruling applies only to weighed items (mawzun); for unique items (qimi), the purchase is entirely for the agent by consensus. Also, only large excess matters; small excess (e.g., half ratl) is negligible and binds the principal.
If quality differs (e.g., lean meat), the purchase is all for the agent by consensus.
5
Determine the tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the foundational position in the school.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah presents it as the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وكله بشراء عشرة أرطال لحم بدرهم، فاشترى عشرين رطلاً مما يباع مثله عشرة بدرهم لزم الموكل منه عشرة بنصف درهم. قيد بالموزون؛ لأنه في القيمي لا ينفذ شيء على الموكل إجماعاً. وقوله: "فاشترى عشرين" قيد بالزيادة الكثيرة؛ لأن القليلة كعشرة أرطال ونصف رطل لازمة للآمر؛ لأنها تدخل بين الوزنين فلا يتحقق حصول الزيادة. وقوله: "لزم الموكل منه عشرة بنصف درهم" أي عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يلزمه العشرون بدرهم؛ لأنه فعل ما أمر به، وزاده خيراً، وبه قالت الثلاثة، كما إذا أمره ببيع عبده بألف فباعه بألفين، وله أنه أمره بشراء عشرة، ولم يأمره بأكثر، فينفذ الزائد على الوكيل بخلاف ما استشهدا به؛ لأن الزيادة فيه بدل ملكه. وقيد بما يباع عشرة بدرهم؛ لأنه إذا كانت عشرة أرطال منه لا تساوي درهماً نفذ الكل على الوكيل بالإجماع؛ لوجود المخالفة؛ لأن الأمر يتناول السمين وما اشتراه مهزول، فلم يحصل مقصود الآمر.
If he appointed him to buy ten ratls of meat for one dirham, and he bought twenty ratls of the same type that sells for ten ratls per dirham, then the principal is bound for ten ratls for half a dirham. Restriction to weighed items because for non-fungibles nothing binds the principal by consensus. His statement 'he bought twenty' is restricted to large excess, because small excess like ten and a half ratls is binding on the principal since it falls between the two weights and the excess is not realized. His statement 'the principal is bound for ten ratls for half a dirham' is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the principal is bound for twenty ratls for one dirham, because he did what he was ordered and added good, and the three said similarly, as if he ordered him to sell his slave for one thousand and he sold him for two thousand. But he (Abu Hanifa) said that he ordered him to buy ten, not more, so the excess is executed for the agent, unlike the example they cited, because the increase there is a substitute for his property. It is restricted to what is sold at ten per dirham, because if ten ratls of it are not worth a dirham, then all binds the agent by consensus due to opposition, because the order includes the fat meat and he bought lean, so the principal's intent was not achieved.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an agent who exceeds the specified quantity in purchasing a weighed (mawzun) item. The ruling: if the agent buys more than instructed (e.g., 20 ratls instead of 10) of a commodity that sells by weight, Abu Hanifa holds that the principal is only bound for the instructed quantity at a proportional price (10 ratls for 0.5 dirham), and the excess is for the agent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the entire purchase is for the principal because the agent acted in his interest by adding good. Abu Hanifa’s proof: the order was limited to ten, and the agent exceeded it, so the excess is a violation; the analogy to selling at a higher price is false because the increase there is a direct benefit to the principal's property. The text also conditions that the excess must be significant; a small excess (e.g., half a ratl) is considered negligible and still binds the principal. Additionally, if the quality differs (e.g., the purchased meat is leaner and worth less), the purchase is entirely for the agent by consensus because the principal's objective was not met.

Key Terms
مَوْزُون (mawzūn): An item sold by weight, considered mithli (fungible) in Hanafi fiqh.
قِيمِيّ (qīmī): A non-fungible item with unique value, cannot be substituted by weight or measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if an agent is told to buy 10 ratls of meat for 1 dirham, and he buys 20 ratls of the same quality, how much is the principal obligated for?
A) 20 ratls for 1 dirham
B) 10 ratls for 0.5 dirham
C) Nothing, because the agent violated the order
D) 10 ratls for 1 dirham

Key Takeaway
When an agent buys more than instructed of a weighed fungible item, the principal is only liable for the specified quantity at the proportional price, per Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view.

Agent Buying a Specifically Identified Item
Ruling that agent cannot buy specifically identified item for himself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent is appointed to buy a specific item and determine for whom the purchase is made, regardless of the agent's intention.

Core Idea
When the item is specifically identified, the agent's purchase is always for the principal—intention doesn't change ownership.

When you appoint someone to buy a particular item—like 'this horse' or 'that slave'—the agency is tied to that specific thing. This lesson explains a straightforward rule: even if the agent tries to buy it for himself, the purchase belongs to you. The method of identification (pointing, naming, or attributing) doesn't matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: the agent cannot buy the specifically identified item for himself. Any purchase of that item is automatically for the principal.
2
Understand the Rationale
The agent's acceptance of the agency creates a commitment that whatever purchase occurs for that specific item belongs to the principal. This makes it impossible for the agent to own it.
3
Note the Irrelevance of Intention
Even if the agent intends to buy for himself or explicitly states it, the purchase still goes to the principal. The agency overrides personal intention.
4
Recognize the Scope of Identification
The ruling applies whether the item is identified by pointing, by name, or by its owner's name. The method does not affect the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وكله بشراء شيء بعينه، ليس للوكيل أن يشتريه لنفسه؛ لأنه بقبول الوكالة التزم أن ما يوجد من شرائه لهذا العين فهو للموكل، فلا يتصور أن يشتريه لنفسه، بل لو اشتراه ينوي بالشراء لنفسه أو تلفظ بذلك يكون للموكل. سواء كان تعيينه بالإشارة أو باسمه العلم أو بالإضافة إلى مالكه.
And if he appointed him to buy a specific item, the agent may not buy it for himself, because by accepting the agency he undertook that whatever occurs of purchase of this specific item is for the principal, so it is not conceivable that he buys it for himself. Rather, if he buys it intending it for himself or explicitly states that, it is for the principal, whether the specification was by pointing, by proper name, or by attribution to its owner.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This is a clear ruling from the matn that when the item to be purchased is specifically identified (e.g., 'this slave' or 'that horse'), the agent cannot buy it for himself. The very nature of the agency makes the purchase automatically for the principal because the agent committed to acquire that specific item for the principal. Even if the agent intends or states it is for himself, the purchase is still for the principal. The method of identification (pointing, naming, or attributing to the owner) does not affect this ruling.

Key Terms
شَيْء بِعَيْنِه (shay’ bi-‘aynihi): A specifically identified item, e.g., a particular slave or animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent is told to buy a specific horse (e.g., 'this horse'), and he purchases it intending it for himself, who owns the horse?
A) The agent, because he intended it for himself
B) The principal, because the agent was appointed to buy it for him
C) The seller can choose
D) Neither, the contract is void

Key Takeaway
When an agent is appointed to buy a specifically identified item, the purchase always belongs to the principal, even if the agent intends or states otherwise.

Agent Buying with Different Currency or Price
Ruling on agent deviating from specified currency or price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when an agent's purchase deviates from the principal's instructions regarding currency type or price, and apply it to determine the validity and ownership of the purchase.

Core Idea
An agent's deviation from the specified currency or price type voids the agency and makes the purchase his own.

In agency contracts, the agent must follow the principal's instructions regarding the medium of payment and the price. However, what happens if the agent chooses a different currency or a different amount? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies when the purchase binds the principal and when it falls to the agent, along with the effect on the agency itself. Understanding these rules is crucial for anyone engaged in trade through representatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of deviation
The passage covers deviations in the medium of payment (currency/goods vs. specified currency) and in the price (higher or lower than specified).
2
Apply the rule for medium deviation
If the agent buys with goods (barter) or a different currency (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams), the purchase is for the agent, not the principal.
This is opposition in the type of price.
3
Apply the rule for price deviation
If the agent pays a higher price than specified with the same currency, the purchase is for the agent. If he pays a lower price, it is a beneficial deviation and binds the principal.
Lower price with same currency is 'khilaf ila khayr' (deviation towards good).
4
Determine the status of the agency
When the purchase falls to the agent due to opposition, the agent is also dismissed from the agency as a result of the violation.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The key factor is whether the agent opposed a fundamental aspect of the order. Changing the type of price (currency or goods) is always opposition; changing the amount only matters if it is detrimental to the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو اشتراه بغير النقود أو بخلاف ما سمى له من الثمن وقع للوكيل. وقوله: "أو بخلاف ما سمى له من الثمن" إن كان أزيد من المسمى، فإن اشتراه بأقل من المسمى بأن وكله بالشراء بمائة، فاشتراه بتسعين، نفذ على الآمر؛ لأنه خلاف إلى خير. غير أنه إذا اختلف الجنس بأن أمره بالدراهم واشترى بالدنانير يصير للوكيل؛ لأنه خالف أمره في الصورتين فينفذ العقد عليه، وينعزل في ضمن المخالفة.
So if he bought it with other than currency or contrary to what was specified as the price, it falls to the agent. And his statement: "or contrary to what was specified as the price" — if it is more than the specified, but if he bought it for less than the specified, e.g., he appointed him to buy for one hundred and he bought for ninety, it is executed for the principal because it is a deviation towards good. However, if the type differs, such as he ordered him to use dirhams and he bought with dinars, it becomes for the agent, because he opposed his order in both cases, so the contract is executed for him and he is dismissed within the opposition.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This ruling addresses the agent's deviation from instructions regarding the medium of payment or the price. If the agent buys with goods (barter) instead of currency, or if the price exceeds what was specified, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal. However, buying at a lower price using the same currency is permissible and binds the principal because it is a beneficial deviation. But if the currency type changes (e.g., dirhams vs. dinars), the purchase is for the agent even if the amount is lower, because the agent fundamentally opposed the order. The agent is also considered dismissed from the agency by such opposition.

Key Terms
نَقْد (naqd): Currency (gold or silver coins) used as payment.
دراهم (darāhim): Silver coins; a specific currency type in classical Hanafi fiqh.
دنانير (danānīr): Gold coins; another specific currency type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent is told to buy an item for 100 dirhams. He buys it for 90 dinars (a different currency). Who is the purchase for?
A) The principal, because it is a lower price
B) The agent, because he used a different currency
C) The principal, because the agent acted in good faith
D) The seller can choose

Key Takeaway
A change in the type of currency always makes the purchase for the agent; a change in the price only matters if it is higher.

Agent Buying Unspecified Item: Intention and Attribution
Ruling on purchase of unspecified item and the effect of intention and money attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine who owns an item purchased by an agent when the item is unspecified, by analyzing the attribution of the contract and the agent's intention according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ownership of an unspecified purchase by an agent depends on whether the contract is attributed to the principal's money, the agent's money, or left ambiguous—then intention decides.

When an agent is authorized to buy an unspecified item, the default ruling is that the purchase is for the agent. However, Hanafi fiqh provides detailed exceptions based on how the agent expresses the purchase and his inner intention. This lesson breaks down three scenarios to clarify ownership, using legal reasoning based on custom and the prohibition of misappropriating another's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The default ruling: if the item is unspecified, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal. Understand that the agent has discretion, so the item could be for himself unless evidence indicates otherwise.
2
Note the exceptions
The agent may intend the purchase for the principal or use the principal's property to pay. In either case, the purchase is for the principal. Recognize that these exceptions override the default.
3
Parse the three attribution scenarios
The text divides the case based on how the agent phrases the contract: (1) attributes to principal's money, (2) to his own money, (3) to absolute dirhams. For each, the ruling differs. Analyze each scenario and the reasoning behind it.
4
Apply the reasoning
For attribution to principal's money: it is legally and customarily objectionable for the agent to buy for himself using another's money, so we interpret the contract in favor of the principal. For attribution to own money: customary practice supports that the owner of the money is the buyer. For absolute dirhams: the agent's intention at the time of purchase becomes decisive because the contract is ambiguous.
5
Determine the effect of intention in ambiguous cases
When the contract is silent on the source of funds (absolute dirhams), the agent's inner intention determines ownership. If he intended the purchase for the principal, it belongs to the principal; if for himself, it is his own.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان بغير عينه فالشراء للوكيل إلا أن ينوي للموكل أو يشتريه بماله. والمسألة على وجوه: إما أن يضيف العقد إلى مال الموكل أو إلى مال نفسه أو إلى دراهم مطلقة. فإن كان الأول كان للأمر، حملاً لحال الوكيل على ما لا يحل له شرعاً؛ إذ الشراء لنفسه بإضافة العقد إلى دراهم غيره مستنكر شرعاً وعرفاً. وإن كان الثاني كان للمأمور، حملاً لفعله على ما يفعله الناس عادة. وإن كان الثالث فيرجع إلى نيته، فإن نواها للأمر فهو للآمر، وإن نواها لنفسه فلنفسه.
And if it is unspecified, then the purchase is for the agent unless he intends it for the principal or buys it with the principal's property. The matter has several aspects: either he attributes the contract to the principal's property, or to his own property, or to absolute dirhams. If the first (attribution to principal's property), it is for the principal, carrying the agent's state to what is not permissible for him legally, because buying for himself by attributing the contract to another's money is objectionable legally and customarily. If the second (attribution to his own property), it is for the agent (the person commanded), carrying his action to what people usually do. If the third (absolute dirhams), it depends on his intention: if he intended it for the principal, it is for the principal; if he intended it for himself, it is for himself.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage parses the ruling for purchasing an unspecified item (e.g., 'buy a slave' without specifying which). The default rule: the purchase is for the agent, not the principal, because the agent was given discretion and the purchase could be for himself. However, there are exceptions: (1) if the agent explicitly intends to buy for the principal, or (2) if he uses the principal's money (attributing the purchase to the principal's wealth). The text then breaks down three scenarios based on how the agent phrases the contract: if he says 'I buy this with the money of the principal,' the purchase is for the principal; if he says 'with my money,' it is for the agent; if he leaves it ambiguous ('with dirhams'), it depends on his inner intention. The reasoning for the first case is that it would be legally and customarily reprehensible for the agent to buy for himself while attributing to the principal's money, so we interpret his action in favor of the principal. For the second case, the customary practice supports that the buyer is the owner of the money. For the third, his intention determines ownership.

Key Terms
مَال (māl): Property; specifically the principal's wealth used for payment.
نِيَّة (niyya): Intention; in this case, the agent's inner resolve at the time of purchase determines ownership when the contract is ambiguous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent is told to buy an unspecified item. He buys a horse saying 'I buy this with dirhams' without specifying whose, and later claims he intended it for the principal. According to the passage, who owns the horse?
A) The agent, because his spoken words determine ownership.
B) The principal, because the agent's intention is decisive in this scenario.
C) The horse is jointly owned.
D) The sale is void due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous agency purchases, the attribution of the contract and the agent's intention are the decisive factors for ownership.
